SE            BRE
HD            Electronics companies fall under EU spotlight
WC           405 words
PD            8 November 2012
SN            Cape Times
SC            CAPTIM
ED            E1
PG            30
LA            English
CY            © 2012 Independent Newspapers (Pty) Ltd

LP
LG Electronics, Panasonic and Royal Philips Electronics might be  fined by EU antitrust regulators within weeks over price-fixing  agreements for cathode-ray  tubes used in televisions, four people said.
Technicolor was also among firms earmarked for possible European Commissionfines as soon as
November 28, one of the people said yesterday.

TD
Other manufacturers including Toshiba had been asked to meet with EU officials prior to a preliminary decision on fines by a panel of EU and national competition authorities, another person said. The people spoke on condition of anonymity because the process is not public.

Sales of cathode-ray  tubes used in TVs and  computer monitors fell after customers switched to slimmer liquid-crystal and plasma display sets. Philips and Technicolor, previously known as Thomson, received objections in the EU probe in 2009.

Antitrust watchdogs in the EU, Japan and South Korea raided companies in 2007 over concerns they colluded to fix prices for  tubes in computer monitors and colour TVs.
The commission can fine cartel members as much as 10 percent of their annual sales. Antoine
Colombani, a spokesman for the Brussels-based authority, declined to comment on the matter.

Joost Akkermans, a spokesman for Amsterdam-based Philips, declined to comment because the investigation was ongoing. Technicolor, based in Paris, and Tokyo-based Toshiba declined to immediately comment.
LG and Panasonic did not immediately respond to calls and e-mails seeking comment.

Chunghwa Picture Tubes would not be fined because it was the first to inform regulators of the  cartel, two people said. Chunghwa did not immediately respond to calls and |e-mails seeking comment.

Hitachi and Samsung SDI, an affiliate of Samsung Electronics, were also involved in the case, one person said.
Hitachi and Samsung did not immediately respond to calls and e-mails.

Philips and LG said in June that they had received supplementary objections from regulators. The EU made the complaint to hold them responsible for the behaviour of LG.Philips Displays, a now-defunct joint venture that made the  tubes, two people familiar with the matter said at the time. LG.Philips Displays' European holding company was declared bankrupt in 2006.

LG Display, partly owned by Seoul-based LG Electronics, was among companies  fined e648.9 million (R7 billion) by the EU in 2010 for  fixing  the price of liquid-crystal display panels used in TVs, computer monitors and notebook computers. - Bloomberg

HD               WSJ: Electronics Firms Face EU Fines
BY               By Vanessa Mock
WC              428 words
PD               9 November 2012
ET                07:29 PM
SN               Dow Jones News Service
SC               DJ
LA                English
CY               (c) 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

LP
BRUSSELS--Royal Philips Electronics NV (PHG, PHIA.AE), Panasonic Corp. (PC, 6752.TO), LG Electronics Inc. (066570.SE) and three other firms are likely to be slapped with stinging European Unionfines later this month, marking the end game in a long-running probe into the price  fixing  of cathode-ray tubes used in televisions and  computer monitors.

TD
Three people said an announcement detailing the  fines had been slated for Nov. 28, though the date isn't definitive and could shift by a few days.

"We expect the Commission to take a very aggressive approach and slap a very big  fine on the main offenders," one of the people said.

Other firms involved in the alleged  cartel are Technicolor SA (TCH.FR, TCLRY), Toshiba Corp. (TOSYY, 6502.TO), and Samsung Electronics Co. (SSNHY, 005930.SE). Not all firms are expected to receive the same levels of  fines.
The Taiwanese firm Chunghwa Picture  Tubes Ltd. (2475.TW) was the whistle-blower in the  cartel
and is unlikely to be  fined, the people said.

A spokesman for the  European Commission declined to comment on the case, saying only that it was an ongoing investigation.

Despite the fact that the cathode-ray technology has been superseded by plasma and liquid-crystal screens, the people said they expected the  fines to be substantial owing to the length of the  cartel and because European Union regulators were viewing this as an important, precedent-setting case.

EU regulators filed a formal complaint in 2009 after raiding the companies' premises in late 2007 as part of an ongoing investigation into price  fixing  for TVs and  computer monitors.

The commission, which acts as the EU's antitrust watchdog, views price-fixing  as among the most harmful restrictions of competition and imposes heavy  fines as a matter of policy.  Fines are typically related to the value of sales, depending on the degree of gravity of the infringement, multiplied by the number of years of infringement.

Legal experts point out that the number of EU  fines related to cartels has gone down significantly over the past two years. "So this might be a good time for the Commission to make up for the shortfall," another person said.

The largest  fine imposed on all members of a single  cartel was more than 1.3 billion euros ($1.65 billion) in 2008 on four manufacturers of car glass.
Write to Vanessa Mock at vanessa.mock@wsj.com
Subscribe to WSJ:  http://online.wsj.com?mod=djnwires [ 11-09-12 1329ET ]

BRUSSELS—Royal Philips Electronics NV, Panasonic Corp., LG Electronics and three other firms are likely to be slapped with stinging European Unionfines later this month, marking the end game in a long-running probe into the price  fixing  of cathode-ray tubes used in televisions and  computer monitors.

Three people said an announcement detailing the  fines had been slated for Nov. 28, though the date isn't definitive and could shift by a few days.

TD
"We expect the Commission to take a very aggressive approach and slap a very big  fine on the main offenders," one of the people said.

Other firms involved in the alleged  cartel are Technicolor SA, Toshiba Corp., and Samsung. Not all firms are expected to receive the same levels of  fines.

The Taiwanese firm Chunghwa Picture  Tubes was the whistleblower in the  cartel and is unlikely to be fined, the people said.

A spokesman for the  European Commission declined to comment on the case, saying only that it was an ongoing investigation.

Despite the fact that the cathode-ray technology has been superseded by plasma and liquid-crystal screens, the people said they expected the  fines to be substantial owing to the length of the  cartel and because European Union regulators were viewing this as an important, precedent-setting case.

EU regulators filed a formal complaint in 2009 after raiding the companies' premises in late 2007 as part of an ongoing investigation into price  fixing  for TVs and  computer monitors.

The commission, which acts as the EU's antitrust watchdog, views price-fixing  as among the most harmful restrictions of competition and imposes heavy  fines as a matter of policy.  Fines are typically related to the value of sales, depending on the degree of gravity of the infringement, multiplied by the number of years of infringement.

Legal experts point out that the number of EU  fines related to cartels has gone down significantly over the past two years. "So this might be a good time for the Commission to make up for the shortfall," another person said.

The largest  fine imposed on all members of a single  cartel was more than €1.3 billion in 2008 on four manufacturers of car glass.

HD          UPDATE 1-Philips,  LG Electronics, others face EU cartel fines
WC         426 words
PD          9 November 2012
ET          04:29 PM
SN          Reuters News
SC          LBA
LA           English
CY          (c) 2012 Reuters Limited

LP
* Samsung SDI, Thomson, Matsushita, Toshiba also face  fines
* Chunghwa Picture  Tubes blew whistle, will not be  fined

TD
* Two other JVs between four of the firms face  fines
(Adds details)
By Foo Yun Chee

BRUSSELS, Nov 9 (Reuters) - Six top electronics firms including Philips and LG Electronics face hefty European Unionfines at the end of the month for  fixing  prices of TV cathode-ray tubes, four people with knowledge of the matter said.

The sources said on Friday other companies involved in the  cartel were South Korea's Samsung SDI, French group Thomson, which was renamed Technicolor in 2010, and Japanese companies Matsushita, now known as Panasonic Corp , and Toshiba Corp.
The European Commission, which raided the companies in late 2007, will announce the  fines on
Nov. 28, according to a Commission document seen by Reuters and confirmed by sources.
Sanctions are expected to be substantial because the  cartel lasted more than a decade from the late
1990s, one of the sources said. The EU executive can penalise companies up to 10 percent of their
turnover for breaching EU rules.

In the case of Dutch group Philips that could reach 2.26 billion euros ($2.9 billion), while for South Korea's LG Electronics it could be 5.4 trillion won ($5.0 billion), based on their 2011 revenues. However, sanctions are not expected to hit those levels.
The sources said Taiwanese company Chunghwa Picture  Tubes
alerted the EU antitrust regulators to the existence of the  cartel, so will not be  fined.

LG Philips Display, a joint venture between Philips and LG Electronics, and MT Picture Display, a joint venture between Matsushita and Toshiba, will also be penalised, the sources said.
The European Commission did not respond to a phone call or email seeking comment. Cathode ray tubes, which were also used in  computer monitors, have largely been replaced by
modern display technologies such as LCD, plasma display and organic light-emitting diode.

The EU authority slapped a total  fine of 648 million euros on six LCD companies which included Chunghwa Picture  Tubes, Samsung Electronics and LG Display two years ago for taking part in a cartel.
Last year, it penalised four producers of cathode ray  tubes glass 128.74 million euros for  fixing  prices.

HD            Corporate  News: EU Price-Fixing Fines Likely
BY             By Vanessa Mock
WC           301 words
PD            12 November 2012
SN            The Wall Street Journal
SC            J
PG            B2
LA             English
CY            (Copyright (c) 2012, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

BRUSSELS -- Royal Philips Electronics NV, Panasonic Corp., LG Electronics and three other firms are likely to be slapped with European Unionfines later this month, marking the end to a long-running probe into the price  fixing  of cathode-ray tubes used in television sets and computer monitors.

Three people said an announcement detailing the  fines had been slated for Nov. 28, though the date wasn't definitive and could shift by a few days.

TD
"We expect the commission to take a very aggressive approach and slap a very big  fine on the main offenders," one of the people said.
Other companies involved in the alleged  cartel are Technicolor SA, Toshiba Corp., and Samsung
Electronics Co. Not all companies are expected to receive the same levels of  fines.

Taiwan's Chunghwa Picture  Tubes was the whistleblower in the  cartel and is unlikely to be  fined, the people said.

A spokesman for the  European Commission declined to comment on the case, saying only that it was an ongoing investigation.

Despite the fact that the cathode-ray technology has been superseded by plasma and liquid-crystal screens, the people said they expected the  fines to be substantial owing to the length of the  cartel and because EU regulators were viewing this as a precedent-setting case.

EU regulators filed a formal complaint in 2009 after raiding the companies' premises in late 2007 as part of an ongoing investigation into price  fixing  for TVs and  computer monitors.

The commission, which acts as the EU's antitrust watchdog, views price-fixing  as among the most harmful restrictions of competition and imposes heavy  fines as a matter of policy.

TWITTER RESETS USERS’ PASSWORDS BY MISTAKE

TWITTER said it mistakenly reset the passwords of “a large number” of its more than 140 million active users while conducting routine security screening to identify accounts that may have been compromised.

TD
A spokesman for the social- networking site declined to say how many Twitter accounts were affected by the error. She added that there had not been a security breach.

Twitter also apologised for any confusion caused by the password resets. Internet-security specialists advise people to regularly change passwords to accounts to thwart hackers.
Twitter also posted a “bug alert”, saying it was working to fix problems with disappearing “tweets”
and direct messages.
REUTERS, AFP
EU TO  FINE SIX FIRMS FOR PRICE  FIXING
SIX top electronics companies, including Philips and LG Electronics, face hefty European
Unionfines for fixing  the prices of  TV cathode-ray tubes.

Four sources said the other firms involved in the  cartel were South Korea’s Samsung, French group Thomson, which was renamed Technicolor in 2010, and Japanese firms Matsushita – now known as Panasonic – as well as Toshiba.
The European Commission, which raided the firms in late 2007, will announce the  fines on Nov
28, according to a commis- sion document.

The sanctions are expected to be substantial as the  cartel lasted more than a decade, from the late 1990s, one of the sources said. The EU executive can penalise firms by up to 10 per cent of their turnover for breaching EU rules.

NEW LCD PANEL SEEN AS A CLEAR WINNER

JAPAN Display said it has developed a prototype LCD panel that has the world’s highest resolution of 651ppi.

Technology-news website HardwareZone reported that the display has a resolution of 1,280x800 pixels packed into a screen size of 2.3 inches.
This is said to be double that of many high-definition smart- phone displays.

“At that level, sensitivity becomes relevant. So, for example, objects that appear three-dimensional look extremely clear.
“We think that, if we give displays even higher resolution, images may look more realistic.”

HD      Czech Republic: Competition and price regulations
WC     2,071 words
PD      1 December 2012
SN      Economist Intelligence Unit - ViewsWire
SC      EIUCP
ED      ViewsWire
PG      262
LA       English
CY      (C) 2012 The Economist Intelligence Unit Ltd.

LP
Competition policy: Overview

Czech competition policy is set out in the Act on the Protection of Competition (Coll 143/2001), which united pre-existing codes and permits practices to mirror European Union convention. The act defines the following practices as unethical: abuse of a dominant market position;  cartel agreements; collusion and trust practices; consumer deception; and price  fixing.  Similar to statutes in other countries, the legislation permits authorities to act with leniency in such cases; hence, those who voluntarily admit to competition violations-such as cartel agreements-are eligible for reductions in penalties of as much as 75%. If the forthcoming entity can prove it did not initiate the  cartel agreement, potential  fines may be thrown out altogether.

TD
The Office for the Protection of Competition (Urad pro ochranu a hospodarske souteze-UOHS), based in Brno, administers the act. The UOHS, formerly the Ministry of Economic Competition, is an autonomous entity. All mergers need to receive its authorisation.

Once accused of not fully exercising its considerable powers, the UOHS has stepped up its activities in recent years. The office imposed  fines totalling Kc4.1bn over 2005-11-and mostly over mispricing. The UOHS issued  fines worth Kc28.8m in 2011, on par with the Kc29m it issued in 2010. The agency launched 89 proceedings in 2011, versus 60 in 2010. Appeals are commonplace in anti-monopoly adjudication, often lasting more than five years.

In October 2012 an administrative court raised a  fine levied in 2004 by the UOHS on SPT Telecom, the former state-held telephone operator, for abusing its monopoly position. Initially  fined Kc81.7m, the court increased the figure to Kc93m and ruled that Telefónica (a Spanish communications giant), which bought the company in a 2005 privatisation, wilfully misled investigators in the inquiry. Telefónica owns 69% of the Prague-listed utility, now known as Telefónica O2, and said it would appeal.

In June 2012 an administrative court struck down a Kc941m  fine, the UOHS's largest-ever, that was levelled in April 2007 against 16 industrial companies, including subsidiaries of multinationals. The companies had participated in what the UOHS said was a  cartel that fixed the prices when bidding on contracts for the installation of natural-gas pipeline-switching systems, both in the Czech Republic and abroad, from 1988-2004. The European Court of Justice ruled affirmatively earlier in the year on the validity of the  fine. Yet although a Czech administrative court based in the Moravian capital of Brno agreed on the existence of a  cartel among the group, it failed to validate the  fine because, the court said, it could not determine the extent to which each company participated in the bid-rigging.

Other noteworthy recent decisions involving international companies include a Kc29.3m settlement reached in February 2011 with consumer-products giants Henkel (Germany), Procter & Gamble (US) and Reckitt Benckiser (UK) on charges of pricing collusion in the sale of detergents, fabric softeners and hand dish cleaners during 1999-2004. Similarly, in September 2010 the office found an array of
cathode-ray-tube makers, including Panasonic and Samsung of Japan, Chunghwa of Taiwan, Philips Electronics of the Netherlands, and LG Electronics of South Korea, had colluded in the market for television colour-picture tubes over 1998-2004, and it levelled a  fine of Kc51.8m against the companies and several subsidiaries.

Page 20 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Competition policy: Monopolies and market dominance

The Act on Protection of Economic Competition (No. 143/1991) does not directly prohibit monopolies or market dominance. Rather, and since most enterprises were state controlled and enjoyed protected markets at the time of its 1991 passage, it focuses on the prohibition of "abuse of market dominance" by companies with large shares of a given marketplace or economic sector. In practical terms, this approach enables some companies-such as Ceske Energeticke Zavody (CEZ), the state-controlled electricity provider-to retain their monopolies while facing regulatory scrutiny for pricing and service delivery. As such, the UOHS ruled in March 2011 that a plan to unite CSA (state-owned airline) with Prague Airport Authority in a holding company structure was sufficient to prevent abuse. Ownership of CSA was transferred to Czech Aeroholding, a state-owned holding firm, in September 2012.

Market dominance is defined as a combination of two factors, both of which must be present to constitute abuse. These are a dominant market share (control of at least 40% of a market sector) and a willingness to misuse this dominance (a concept that lacks clear legal definition). Enterprises with a dominant market share must report their status to the Office for the Protection of Competition (Urad pro ochranu a hospodarske souteze-UOHS), the Czech agency that is charged with regulating competition.

Parliament amended the competition law in 2001 to require the UOHS to examine any merger or takeover that involves a company with sales exceeding Kc550m. Enacted in July of that year, the amendment mandates a two-stage procedure whereby an initial one-month examination may be followed by a five-month, in-depth investigation if warranted. The amendment also allows interest groups and
other affected organisations to give their opinion on a merger. Mergers can be frozen until the entire investigation is completed. A 2009 amendment simplified the approvals process for mergers not involving the threat of "distortion of market competition" (that is, for vertical integration). The changes shortened
the evaluation period to 20 days, enable electronic notifications and decisions, and bring Czech law more closely in line with European Union competition directives.

In 2003 the Czech parliament enacted legislation to ban  cartel agreements, even those considered to be "insignificant". (An insignificant agreement is one in which the parties to the agreement together control less than 5% of the market for their goods or services.)

Monopolies per se are not illegal under Czech law, and some do exist. One is RWE Transgas, the formerly state-owned natural-gas importer sold in 2002 to RWE of Germany for EUR4.1bn. In 1997 the Ministry of Industry and Trade set licensing restrictions for the import of natural gas that required applicants to possess a high-pressure gas and water system capable of supplying gas throughout the entire country. Transgas was-and is-the only company that meets the requirement. The company was further strengthened when eight regional gas distributors were rolled into it ahead of privatisation.

In 2006 RWE Transgas was  fined Kc370m for abuse of market dominance (later reduced to Kc240m since RWE Transgas co-operated with the UOHS and amended some of the contested contractual arrangements). RWE Transgas appealed, and a regional court rejected the  fine on procedural grounds in the third quarter of 2007. The UOHS appealed against the regional court's decision to the Supreme Administrative Court, which ruled in 2008 that the UOHS's procedure was correct, transferring the case back to the regional court to decide once again on the material substance (that is, abuse of market position). The regional court ruled a year later, in October 2009, that the UOHS had erred in its definition of the "relevant market". The court returned a subsequent appeal to the lower court in September 2011, and no date had then been set for a new hearing.

In an unrelated matter, the UHOS announced in June 2009 that RWE Transgas would pay Kc10m to settle charges stemming from a September 2008 investigation that the company overcharged more than
76,000 customers by as much as 10% in an advance-billing scheme-70% of the  fine is to compensate affected consumers. In addition, it said RWE Transgas would free some 20% of underground-storage capacity for independent suppliers lest it be accused of abusing its dominant position in the Czech natural-gas marketplace.
Competition policy: Mergers

The Act on the Transformation of Business Companies and Co-operatives (Col 125/2008), implemented in July 2008, aligns Czech legislation with a 2005 EU directive governing crossborder merger of
limited-liability companies. The act governs national and international mergers conducted via the formation of a new company and via acquisition. The law permits takeovers of both listed and non-listed companies, enabling the holder(s) of 90% of a company's shares to buy out minority shareholders; for contested bids, the takeover is at a price determined by a court-appointed arbitrator.

For competition purposes, the Office for the Protection of Competition (Urad pro ochranu a hospodarske souteze-UOHS) places the same constraints on mergers as on all companies. The government has

Page 21 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
never openly forbidden a merger, and there have been no major court cases involving mergers. An amendment in September 2009 to the Act on Protection of Economic Competition (Col 143/2001) streamlined approval processes for mergers that do not lead to market dominance, such as vertical integrations. The simplified procedure cuts the evaluation period to 20 days (from 30), and enables electronic notifications and decisions, thus aligning Czech law more closely with EU directives. And in September 2011 CzechInvest, the government's investment-promotion agency, launched an internet portal to foster mergers of domestic companies with foreign partners.
Competition policy: Freedom to sell

Companies face no formal legal restrictions on their freedom to sell. However, recent actions on the part of the Office for the Protection of Competition (Urad pro ochranu a hospodarske souteze-UOHS) indicate that this freedom might be curtailed somewhat when companies hold dominant market positions.

Companies are free to set up exclusive distributorships but are generally not permitted to force retailers into restrictive selling arrangements. Producers are not bound in principle to any particular distribution channel and are free to offer discounts on bulk orders and other types of pricing incentives. But they are expected to treat customers equally and to refrain from price discrimination. In January 2010 the UOHS fined Karlovarske mineralni vody (a bottler) Kc5m for restraint of trade in export-distribution agreements. In September 2010 the office  finedSokolovska uhelna (a coal company) nearly Kc17.3m, after ruling that its distribution agreements limiting export affected the local-market price for brown-coal briquettes and other products.

It is technically possible to sue an unauthorised dealer. In practice, however, this almost never happens since the congested court system is notoriously slow.
Competition policy: Price controls

The Price Law (No. 526/1990), enacted in January 1991, governs price controls and gives the government broad powers to regulate prices. The Ministry of Finance (Ministerstvo financi)-and for pharmaceuticals, the Ministry of Health-can fix prices directly, set minimum or maximum prices for any commercial transaction, and establish periods when prices may not change. The ministry may require sellers deemed to be in a position of market dominance to give a one-month notice of any planned price changes. The government must publish a list of goods and services for which price regulations apply.

Despite its authority, the government favours a laissez-faire pricing policy on most products. Goods and services that are subject to controls include energy, some raw materials, pharmaceuticals, domestic rents, and rail and bus transport. Maximum prices also apply on mail and telecommunications tariffs.

The three-stage liberalisation of the electricity market, begun in 1999 by the Ministry of Industry and Trade (Ministerstvo prumyslu a obchodu), reached completion in 2006, when customers could choose a supplier. The gas market was liberalised in 2007, when first businesses and then households could choose their supplier, though the state regulator still set maximum prices. Imports are not subject to price controls.

In December 2012 the Czech Telecommunication Office (CTU), the market regulator, proposed establishing price controls for network access as a means of encouraging greater competition in the mobile-telephony market. By forcing the three owners of the country's network operators
licences-T-Mobile (Germany), Telefónica (Spain) and Vodafone (UK)-to make public their wholesale pricing policies, the CTU hopes to encourage so-called virtual operators to enter the market. Virtual providers, which would lease capacity from network operators, would boost competition by offering services at a cost lower than that offered by the major providers. Following a period of public comment, expected to close at the end of January 2013, the CTU and Office for the Protection of Competition (Urad pro ochranu a hospodarske souteze-UOHS) will jointly prepare a draft policy to be advanced to the European Commission ahead of implementation, anticipated in early in 2014.

HD     EU slaps 1.47-billion-euro fine on cathode ray tube cartel
WC     263 words
PD      5 December 2012
ET      12:10 PM
SN      dpa International Service in English
SC      DPAEN
LA      English
CY      Copyright 2012. dpa Deutsche Presse-Agentur

LP
Brussels (dpa) - The European Union on Wednesday slapped a 1.47-billion-euro (1.92-billion-dollar)  fine on seven electronics companies - including giants Philips, Samsung, LG Electronics and Panasonic - for having illegally formed a cathode ray tube  cartel during the 1990s.

The firms illegally "fixed prices, shared markets, allocated customers between themselves and restricted their output" from 1996 until 2006 in the markets for television and computer cathode ray tubes, according to the EU's executive, the European Commission.

TD
It said the worldwide  cartel was "among the most organized" it had ever investigated. With cathode ray tubes become less and less prevalent, the  cartel participants "were trying to address the decline of the CRT market in a collusive way," the commission said.

"These cartels for cathode ray  tubes ... feature all the worst kinds of anti-competitive behaviour that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe," EU Competition Commissioner Joaquin Almunia said.

"Cathode ray  tubes were a very important component in the making of  television and computer screens. They accounted for 50 to 70 per cent of the price of a screen," he added, arguing that European consumers were harmed as a result.

The commission said that Chunghwa, LG Electronics, Philips and Samsung SDI participated in both cartels, while Panasonic, Toshiba, MTPD and Technicolor participated only in the  television cartel.

Philips and LG Electronics face the largest  fines, while Chunghwa's penalties were waived because it blew the whistle on the  cartel. dpa amh npr Author: Alexandra Mayer-Hohdahl

HD     LEAD: EU slaps record fine on cathode ray tube cartel Eds: Adds record fine, Almunia  quotes, Philips  reax

LP
Brussels (dpa) - The European Union on Wednesday slapped a record 1.47-billion-euro
(1.92-billion-dollar)  fine on seven electronics companies - including giants Philips, Samsung, LG
Electronics and Panasonic - for forming a cathode ray tube (CRT)  cartel during the 1990s.

The firms illegally "fixed prices, shared markets, allocated customers between themselves and restricted their output" from 1996 until 2006 in the markets for  television and computer CRTs, according to the EU's executive, the  European Commission.

TD
"These cartels for cathode ray  tubes ... feature all the worst kinds of anti-competitive behaviour that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe," said EU Competition Commissioner Joaquin Almunia.

"The cartellists knew very well that their conduct was illegal," Almunia added. "This is why they did everything they could to hide it."
"Cathode ray  tubes were a very important component in the making of  television and computer
screens. They accounted for 50 to 70 per cent of the price of a screen," Almunia added.

He said the worldwide  cartel was "among the most organized" the commission had ever investigated, imposing "serious harm" on both manufacturers and private households.
The combined fine was the commission's biggest ever in a  cartel decision, the commissioner said.

With cathode ray  tubes becoming less and less prevalent, the  cartel participants "were trying to address the decline of the CRT market in a collusive way," the commission said in a statement.

The cartellists would hold so-called green meetings in Asia and Europe, Almunia said, explaining that they were "called this way because they took place very often at a golf course."
Other gatherings, referred to by the cartellists as glass meetings, had been the "engine room of the
cartel," according to information from a whistleblower.

"Because of the success of the glass meeting, everybody has been enjoying business this year," Almunia quoted from one company memo.

"The word 'glass' was not because of the objective of being transparent," Almunia responded to speculation on the meaning of the term.

The commission said that Chunghwa, LG Electronics, Philips and Samsung SDI participated in both cartels, while Panasonic, Toshiba, MTPD and Technicolor participated only in the  television cartel.

Philips and LG Electronics face the largest  fines, while Chunghwa's penalties were waived because it blew the whistle on the  cartel.
Philips said in a statement that it intended to appeal its  fines - 313 million euros plus a joint
392-million-euro fine with LG Electronics - calling them "disproportionate and unjustified."

The Dutch-based company said it had transferred its CRT activities in 2001 to a separate joint venture with LG Electronics that had since gone bankrupt.

Page 25 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
"We regret any association with this type of behavior," said Philips chief executive Frans van Houten. "Our ethical standards ... are very clear and must be strictly adhered to."

Technicolor stated that it had made provisions for a  fine, which would feature as an "extraordinary item" in its income statement for the second half of 2012. dpa amh hm npr Authors: Alexandra Mayer-Hohdahl, Helen Maguire

HD     TV, computer screen cartel hit with record EU fine
WC    555 words
PD     5 December 2012
ET     02:59 PM
SN     EurActiv.com SC     EURACOM LA     English
CY     Copyright 2012. EurActiv.com. All Rights Reserved.

LP
Philips, LG Electronics, Samsung SDI and three other firms were fined a record €1.47 billion by EU antitrust regulators on Wednesday (5 December) for  fixing  prices of TV and monitor cathode-ray  tubes for nearly a decade.

TD
<div> <div>  </div> <!-- By use of this code snippet, I agree to the Brightcove Publisher T and C found at https://accounts.brightcove.com/en/terms-and-conditions/. --><script src="http://admin.brightcove.com/js/BrightcoveExperiences.js" type="text/javascript" width="" language="JavaScript" height=""> </script> <object width="" class="BrightcoveExperience" id="myExperience2014813845001" height=""><param name="bgcolor" value="#FFFFFF"><param name="width" value="450"><param name="height" value="250"><param name="playerID" value="784056053001"><param name="playerKey"

The European Commission slapped the biggest penalty, of €313.4 million, on Dutch-based Philips. LG Electronics came in second with a €295.6 million  fine.
The EU competition authority  finedPanasonic Corp €157.5 million, Samsung SDI €150.8 million, Toshiba
Corp. €28 million, and French company Technicolor €38.6 million.

Two Panasonic joint ventures were also  fined. Taiwanese firm Chunghwa Picture  Tubes blew the whistle on the cartels in  TV and computer monitors and escaped a  fine.
Philips announced that it will take a provision of €509 million in the fourth quarter this year to cover the
fine, saying it intends to appeal the decision by the EU's antitrust authorities.

"We believe that the  fine which relates to a business that has been divested in 2001 is disproportionate and unjustified," the company said in a  statement.

The two cartels, one involving TVs and the other  computer monitors, operated worldwide between 1996 and 2006, during which company executives discussed how to fix prices and share markets at "green meetings", so-called because the events often ended with a round of golf.
"These cartels for cathode ray  tubes are 'textbook cartels': they feature all the worst kinds of
anti-competitive behaviour that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe," EU
Competition Commissioner Joaquin Almunia said in a statement.

The companies were well aware they were breaking the law, the Commission said. "For instance, in a document found during the Commission's inspections, a warning goes as follows: 'Everybody is requested to keep it as secret as it would be serious damage if it is open to customers or  European Commission'," the EU executive noted.
He said the violations were especially harmful for consumers, as cathode ray  tubes accounted for 50 to
70% of the price of a screen.

Page 27 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Cathode ray tubes have largely been replaced by more advanced display technologies such as liquid-crystal display (LCD), plasma display and organic light-emitting diodes.

The biggest  fine prior to the cathode-ray tube  cartel was €1.38 billion imposed on participants in a car glass cartel in 2008.

The Commission's sanctions followed a total  fine of €128.74 million levied last year against four producers of the glass used in cathode-ray  tubes.

And Chunghwa Picture  Tubes, Samsung Electronics, LG Display and three other LCD companies were penalised a total €648 million two years ago for taking part in a  cartel.

HD      EU fines producers of TV, computer monitor tubes EUR1.47B for cartels
WC     184 words
PD      5 December 2012
SN      Theflyonthewall.com
SC      FLYWAL
LA      English
CY      (c) 2012. Theflyonthewall.com. All Rights Reserved.

LP
The European Commission has fined seven international groups of companies a total of EUR1.47B for participating in either one or both of two distinct cartels in the sector of cathode ray  tubes. For almost ten years, between 1996 and 2006, these companies fixed prices, shared markets, allocated customers between themselves and restricted their output. One cartel concerned color picture  tubes used for televisions and the other one colour display  tubes used in computer monitors. The cartels operated worldwide. The infringements found by the Commission therefore cover the entire European Economic Area. Chunghwa, LG Electronics, Philips (PHG) and Samsung SDI (SSNLF) participated in both cartels, while Panasonic (PC), Toshiba (TOSBF), MTPD and Technicolor (TCH) participated only in the  cartel for television tubes. Chunghwa received full immunity from  fines under the Commission's 2006 Leniency Notice for the two cartels, as it was the first to reveal their existence to the Commission.

TD
Other companies received reductions of their  fines for their cooperation in the investigation under the
Commission's leniency program.

Page 29 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.


HD       Brussels fines electronics cartel €1.5bn
BY        By Simon Mundy in Seoul and Alex Barker in Brussels
WC       411 words
PD        5 December 2012
SN        Financial Times (FT.Com)
SC        FTCOM
LA        English
CY        Copyright 2012 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web.

LP
European regulators have issued  fines of €1.47bn to seven electronics groups after finding evidence of price-rigging that lasted nearly a decade.

Companies including Philips; Samsung SDI, an arm of the Korean conglomerate; and Panasonic conspired to fix the prices of cathode ray  tubes, used in older televisions and  computer screens, the European Commission said on Wednesday. It said that by restricting output and allocating customers among themselves from 1996 to 2006, the companies pushed up the prices of display screens for European consumers.

TD
Joaquín Almunia, the commission's vice-president in charge of competition policy, said: "These . . . are
'textbook cartels' - they feature all the worst kinds of anti-competitive behaviour that are strictly forbidden
to companies doing business in Europe."
The tubes concerned accounted for up to 70 per cent of the price of a screen, he said.
The aggregate penalty is the most severe ever imposed by the commission in a single decision. It
comes after large fines were levied in both Europe and the US against a number of flat-panel makers for
similar price-fixing  activity from 2001 to 2006. Flat-screen technology replaced more bulky cathode ray
tubes in TVs and monitors around 2000.

Chungwha Picture Tube, of Taiwan, was spared a  fine because it alerted regulators to the existence of the cartels. However,  fines are payable by all the other companies involved, including LG Electronics, Technicolor and MTPD, which is now owned by Panasonic. Philips faces the most severe penalty,
at €450m.

Philips said it would appeal the ruling. Frans van Houten, chief executive, issued a statement calling the fine "disproportionate and unjustified", noting that the company had spun off its cathode ray tube unit into a joint venture with LG in 2001.
The other companies involved could not immediately be reached for comment.

The commission said the two cartels - one for  tubes used in televisions, and the other for  tubes used in computer screens - were among the most organised it had investigated, noting that the companies monitored each others' compliance with capacity restrictions through plant visits.

The price-fixing  policy was decided at "top management level meetings, dubbed 'green(s) meetings' by the alleged cartelists themselves because they were often followed by a golf game", the commission said.

More junior staff held more meetings as often as once a week to determine prices, production and market share.

Page 31 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.


HD        Record €1.47bn fine for TV cartels
BY         By Alex Barker in Brussels and Simon Mundy in Seoul
WC        481 words
PD         5 December 2012
SN         Financial Times (FT.Com)
SC         FTCOM
LA         English
CY         Copyright 2012 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web.

LP
Philips, LG Electronics and Samsung SDI were hit with a record €1.47bn  fine from Europe's top competition enforcer on Wednesday after participating in "textbook cartels" for almost a decade.

Seven electronics groups in total were punished for two distinct conspiracies to rig prices and the supply of cathode ray  tubes, an obsolete technology once used in televisions and  computer monitors.

TD
European Commission investigators found senior managers at self-styled "greens meetings" plotted to carve up worldwide markets and overcharge consumers before often playing golf at exclusive clubs in Singapore, Korea and Japan.
More junior staff then developed the details of the arrangements at sometimes weekly "glass meetings"
- named after the material covering the tubes - where key information was shared and prices set,
sometimes for individual  television and computer manufacturers.

The combined €1.5bn penalty for the two cartels is the biggest  fine imposed by the European               Commission in a single decision and exceeds the €1.4bn levied on a  cartel on glass for car windows.

Joaquín Almunia, the EU's competition chief, said the two conspiracies featured the "worst kinds of anti-competitive behaviour" that caused "serious harm" to  television and computer makers. The
price-fixing,  market-sharing and customer allocation became almost routine business practice between
1996 and 2006.

While the collusion began at the peak of the market for cathode  tubes, it became a way to mitigate the drop-off in sales as customers moved to slim display screens using plasma or liquid crystal.

In one document seized by investigators, a  cartel member describes setting a goal to "control supply and maintain a proper price to survive". Another asks conspirators to keep the arrangements "secret" so the European Commission does not find out. "Please dispose the following document after reading it," it said.

Mr Almunia joked that the glass  cartel was not created with "the objective of being transparent". He said the high fine reflected the long duration of the cartels and the breadth of co-ordination.
Chunghwa of Taiwan won immunity for alerting authorities to the wrongdoing. LG Electronics, Philips
and Samsung SDI, an arm of the Korean conglomerate, participated in both cartels. Panasonic, Toshiba,
MTPD (now a Panasonic subsidiary) and Technicolor only colluded on  television parts.

Philips faces the biggest penalty of €450m. It said it would appeal the "disproportionate and unjustified" fine, noting that the company had spun off its cathode ray tube unit into a joint venture with LG in 2001. Panasonic and Toshiba are also considering mounting legal challenges.

The commission decision comes after large  fines were levied in Europe and the US against a number of flat-panel makers for similar price-fixing  activity from 2001 to 2006. Flat-screen technology replaced more bulky cathode ray tubes in TVs and monitors from about 2000.

BRUSSELS, Dec. 5 -- The European Union on Wednesday imposed a total of 1.47 billion euros (about 160 billion yen) in cartel fines on six companies, including Toshiba Corp. and Panasonic Corp., claiming they fixed prices for television and computer monitor cathode ray  tubes.

TD

LP
BRUSSELS, Dec. 5 -- The European Union on Wednesday imposed record  cartel fines of 1.47 billion euros (about 160 billion yen) on six companies, including Toshiba Corp. and Panasonic Corp.,
claiming they fixed prices globally for  television and computer monitor cathode ray  tubes over 10 years.
The European Commission ruled that a total of seven consumer appliance makers in Asia and
Europe artificially set prices, divided up the market and adjusted production of cathode ray  tubes from
1996 to 2006 in breach of EU competition law, fining six of the seven companies.

TD
A Taiwan company escaped being  fined as it was the first to reveal the  cartel to the European Union. The other companies  fined include Samsung SDI and LG Electronics of South Korea.

The European Commission said in a statement that the companies' actions demonstrated "all the worst kinds of anticompetitive behavior that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe."
Top executives of the companies were involved in the anticompetitive behavior, it said. Panasonic was fined 157.47 million euros, with Toshiba punished with a 28.04 million euro  fine.
Both the Japanese companies questioned the commission's findings. Panasonic said in a statement, "After fully examining the content of the  European Commission's decision, we will consider how to deal with this with an eye to filing a lawsuit with a court of the EU."

Toshiba said it has been asserting that there was no breach of EU competition law with regard to its cathode ray tube business and it is "regrettable" that its argument was rejected.

TV and computer monitor screens have been replaced with liquid crystal and plasma displays, and production of cathode ray  tubes has almost ended.

HD         UPDATE 3-EU imposes  record 1.47 bln euro cartel fine on Philips,  5 others
WC        676 words
PD         5 December 2012
ET         12:17 PM
SN         Reuters News
SC         LBA
LA         English
CY         (c) 2012 Reuters Limited

LP
* Executives fixed prices before playing golf
* Philips fined 313.4 mln euros, LG Electronics 295.6 mln

TD
* Philips to appeal  fine but to make 509 mln euro provision
* Cartels operated worldwide between 1996-2006 (Adds Panasonic comment)
By Foo Yun Chee

BRUSSELS, Dec 5 (Reuters) - The  European Commission imposed the biggest antitrust penalty in its history on Wednesday, fining six firms including Philips, LG Electronics and Panasonic a total of 1.47 billion euros ($1.92 billion) for running two cartels for nearly a decade.

The Commission said executives from the European and Asian companies met until six years ago to fix prices and divide up markets for  TV and computer monitor cathode-ray  tubes, technology now mostly made obsolete by flat screens.

Between 1996 and 2006 they met in Paris, Rome, Amsterdam and in Asia for "green meetings", so-called because they often ended in a round of golf.
"These cartels for cathode-ray  tubes are 'textbook cartels': they feature all the worst kinds of
anti-competitive behaviour that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe," EU
Competition Commissioner Joaquin Almunia said in a statement.

The EU antitrust regulator imposed the biggest penalties on Philips for its role in the price  fixing  and carving up of markets. The Dutch-based firm was  fined 313.4 million euros and faces a further penalty through a joint venture.

However, Chief Executive Frans van Houten said the group would challenge what he called the disproportionate and unjustified penalty.

LG Electronics of South Korea must pay 295.6 million euros plus its share of a joint venture penalty, followed by Panasonic Corp which was  fined 157.5 million.

The Japanese firm said it might also make a legal challenge. "Panasonic believes the EU decision is factually and legally erroneous ... and will carefully review the decision and consider our options for appeal to the European courts," it said.
The Commission also finedSamsung SDI 150.8 million euros, Toshiba Corp. 28 million euros, and
French company Technicolor 38.6 million euros.
A joint venture between Philips and LG Electronics was penalised 391.9 million euros while two
Panasonic joint ventures were also sanctioned.


Page 40 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Almunia said the violations were especially harmful for consumers, as cathode-ray  tubes accounted for
50 to 70 percent of the price of a screen.

Cathode-ray tubes have largely been replaced by more advanced display technologies such as liquid-crystal display (LCD), plasma display and organic light-emitting diodes.

Philips sold off the business which committed the infringement in 2001 but said it would make a provision of 509 million euros in the fourth quarter for the  fine.

ING analyst Fabian Smeets told ANP-Reuters that the sanction was significant, but had been expected. Philips' shares were down 0.82 percent to 19.93 euros at late afternoon, erasing earlier gains after
news of the  fines.

Technicolor said the  fine, which will be booked as an exceptional item in its second-half accounts, would not affect its 2012 earnings and free cash flow targets.

Until now, the Commission's biggest antitrust penalty had been a 1.38 billion euro  fine imposed on participants in a car glass  cartel in 2008.

The Commission's sanctions followed a total fine of 128.74 million euros levied last year against four producers of the glass used in cathode-ray tubes.

Chunghwa Picture  Tubes, Samsung Electronics, LG Display and three other LCD companies were penalised a total 648 million euros two years ago for taking part in a  cartel.
($1 = 0.7642 euros)
(Reporting by Foo Yun Chee, additional reporting by Anthony Deutsch in Amsterdam, Elena Berton in
Paris, Timothy Kelly in Tokyo and Miyoung Kim in Seoul; editing by Rex Merrifield and David Stamp)

European Union anti-trust authorities announced Wednesday a record  fine for running a cartel, ordering seven companies including LG Electronics and Philips to pay nearly 1.5 billion euros ($1.9 billion) for jacking up the prices for TVs and  computer screens for a decade.
"It's the biggest  fine for a cartel decision" by the EU executive, said Competition Commissioner Joaquin
Almunia.

TD
A European Commission spokesman confirmed that the penalty was the biggest since a
1.38-billion-euro 2008 fine for a car glass cartel.

One cartel concerned tubes used for televisions and the other those for  computer monitors. Both operated worldwide.

The companies involved include household names such as LG Electronics, Philips and Samsung, in both cartels, plus Panasonic, Toshiba, and Technicolor (formerly Thomson) in the  TV end.
The Taiwanese company Chunghwa was granted complete immunity and not  fined as it disclosed the
cartel to regulators.
The companies colluded, the Commission concluded, to fix 50-70 percent of the price of these screens. "The European Commission has fined seven international groups of companies a total of
1,470,515,000 for participating in either one or both of two distinct cartels in the sector of cathode ray
tubes," said a statement.

"For almost 10 years, between 1996 and 2006, these companies fixed prices, shared markets, allocated customers between themselves and restricted their output," the Commission said.

The case goes back to a November 2007 raid.

Europe and often sealed their decisions on golf courses, said the Commission.
It added that the investigation showed the companies knew that what they were doing was illegal.

"In a document found during the Commission's inspections, a warning goes as follows: 'Everybody is requested to keep it as secret as it would be serious damage if it is open to customers or  European         Commission'," said a Commission statement.

The industry has already moved away from cathode ray  tubes to make TV and computer, having embraced liquid crystal (LCD) or light emitting diode (LED) technology.

HD      EU hits screen makers with record cartel fine
WC     430 words
PD      5 December 2012

The European Commission on Wednesday hit seven top  TV and computer screen makers, including LG Electronics and Philips, with a record  fine of 1.5 billion euros for running decade-long price-fixing cartels.
"It's the biggest  fine for a cartel decision," EU Competition Commissioner Joaquin Almunia said.

TD
The previous largest such penalty was a 1.38-billion-euro 2008  fine for an auto glass  cartel, a
Commission spokesman said.
The Commission said there were two cartels operating worldwide -- one for  television and another for
computer screens, based on conventional Cathode Ray Tube technology of the time.

The companies involved included household names such as LG Electronics and Samsung of South Korea, and Chunghwa of Taiwan alongside Philips of the Netherlands in both cartels, while Japan's Panasonic and Toshiba, and Technicolor, formerly Thomson, were involved in  TV screens.
Taiwan giant Chunghwa however was granted complete immunity and not  fined as it disclosed the  cartel
to regulators. Other companies got lesser  fines for cooperating in the investigation, the Commission said.

The companies colluded in the supply and pricing of CRTs which accounted for between 50 and 70 percent of the price of the screens.

"The European Commission has fined seven international groups of companies a total of (1.47 billion euros) ... for participating in either one or both of two distinct cartels in the sector of cathode ray  tubes," a statement said.

"For almost 10 years, between 1996 and 2006, these companies fixed prices, shared markets, allocated customers between themselves and restricted their output," it said.

The two cartels were among the most organised that the Commission had investigated, it said, carrying out the "most harmful anti-competitive practices including price  fixing,  market sharing, customer allocation, capacity and output coordination and exchanges of commercial sensitive information."
The investigation also showed that the companies were well aware they were breaking the law.

"In a document found during the Commission's inspections, a warning goes as follows: 'Everybody is requested to keep it as secret as it would be serious damage if it is open to customers or  European         Commission'," the statement said.

The Commission noted too how top management level meetings were dubbed "green(s) meetings" because they were often followed by a golf game.
Almunia said the case was "textbook," illustrating all the worst features of anti-competitive practices. "These cartels for cathode ray  tubes are 'textbook cartels': they feature all the worst kinds of
anti-competitive behaviour that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe."

HD     EU imposes  $1.9 billion cartel fine on screen producers including Philips,  LG, Panasonic

BY      By RAF CASERT CR     Associated Press WC     752 words
PD      5 December 2012
ET      05:05 PM
SN      Associated Press Newswires
SC      APRS
LA      English
CY      (c) 2012. The Associated Press. All Rights Reserved.

LP
BRUSSELS (AP) - For years, some top managers at electronics multinationals would hold secretive meetings in Europe and Asia to fix the global market on the most expensive part of  television and computer monitors.

Then, said the European Union's antitrust chief, it was off to the golf course, where the executives would complete the "green(s) meetings" at which they set up a massive scam that affected millions of consumers.

TD
On Wednesday, EU antitrust Commissioner Joaquin Almunia said he got back at them, slapping the biggest ever cartel fine -- over (EURO)1.47 billion ($1.96 billion) -- on seven companies for allegedly rigging the international market of  television and computer monitor tubes.

The EU's Commission ruled that, for a decade ending in 2006, the companies -- including Philips, LG Electronics and Panasonic -- artificially set prices, shared markets and restricted their output.

Almunia said the companies' actions "feature all the worst kinds of anticompetitive behavior that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe."

Tubes were the essential part of  television screens and computer monitors before they were replaced by LCD and plasma flatscreens. The cathode ray  tubes accounted for up to 70 percent of the cost of a screen, the Commission said.
Almunia added that the  tubes' cost gave "an indication of the serious harm" the companies had caused.
`'There are victims: millions and millions of citizens."

Philips and LG Electronics, which acted jointly and separately, were  fined a combined (EURO)999 million ($1.3 billion) with Panasonic punished with a (EURO)157 million ($205 million)  fine, adding to more if combined fines and affiliates were included.
Despite its cooperation with the Commission probe, Philips said in a statement it planned to appeal the
fine since it considered it `'disproportionate and unjust."

"We regret that we are linked to this kind of behavior," said Philips CEO Frans van Houten, as the company spoke of `'alleged infringements of competition rules."
LG Electronics said it would only react after studying the decision.

Other companies  fined were Samsung SDI, Technicolor, MTPD and Toshiba. Chunghwa of Taiwan escaped fines as it was the first to reveal the  cartel to the EU.
`'It is the biggest  fine for a cartel, ever," Almunia said. The fine exceeded the previous record of the 2008
fine of (EURO)1.38 billion ($1.8 billion) in a car glass cartel.

Almunia said the  tubes cartel operated worldwide and that the companies involved fully realized they acted illegally.
Page 48 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
After top management set the strategy at Green(s) meetings, lower-level company officials discussed the practical applications of the price-fixing  scheme during so-called `'Glass meetings," referring to the core material of the  tubes.

`'The participants during this period seemed very happy about the outcome," Almunia said, citing one document that read: `'Because of the success of the `glass meeting' everybody has been enjoying business this year." Not the consumer, he said.

The cartel started when the tube market was still booming but lasted even when sales dropped with the advent of flatscreens. The  cartel made sure the companies kept making profits even as demand for tube screens waned.

Almunia said the companies sought to keep prices as high as possible before new technologies came to the market. One document seized by the Commission gave the conspiring companies clear instructions: "Producers need to avoid price competition through controlling their production capacity."

A separate document had a warning: "Everybody is requested to keep it as secret as it would be serious damage if it is open to customers or  European Commission." Another said: "Please dispose the following document after reading it."

`'Instead of competing with each other to innovate and provide the best product at the best price, they chose to conspire to artificially maintain returns in a declining technology market," Almunia said. `'This is why we fight cartels."

He said the television and computer cartels were among the most sophisticated the EU had ever dealt with. He said they not only fixed prices, but also shared markets, coordinated capacity, output and customer allocation. In the  computer monitor cartel, they even audited compliance.

The Commission started its probe with an antitrust raid on the companies in November 2007. Almunia said U.S. authorities were currently investigating the case as well.

Mike Corder in The Hague and Sam Kim in Seoul contributed to this article.

HD            European regulator fines 7 global electronics firms $1.92 bn for price fixing
CR            Distributed by Contify.com

LP
The European regulator has  fined seven global electronics firms a record $1.92 billion for  fixing
prices of TV and monitor cathode-ray  tubes for nearly a decade.

Brussels-based European Commission (EC) imposed its largest-ever penalty on Philips, LG Electronics, Samsung SDI, Panasonic, Toshiba and French company Technicolor (formerly Thomson).

TD
Taiwan's Chunghwa Picture Tubes, which blew the whistle on the cartels in  TV and computer monitors was let off without being fined, while other companies received reductions of their fines for cooperating in the investigation.

The EC said that for almost ten years, between 1996 and 2006, these companies fixed prices, shared markets, allocated customers between themselves and restricted their output.

The cartel also monitored the implementation, including auditing compliance with the capacity restrictions by plant visits in the case of the  computer monitor tubes cartel.
One cartel concerned colour picture  tubes used for televisions and the other for colour display  tubes
used in computer monitors.

Chunghwa, LG Electronics, Philips and Samsung SDI were involved in both cartels, while Panasonic, Toshiba, MTPD (currently a Panasonic subsidiary) and Technicolor participated only in the  cartel for television tubes.
The EC said the two CRT cartels are among the most organised cartels that it has investigated. Top management of these companies held meetings, dubbed "green(s) meetings" in a golf course
and designed the plan for the two cartels.
Preparation and implementation were carried out through lower level meetings, often referred to as
"glass meetings", on a quarterly, monthly, sometimes even weekly basis.

Meetings were held in various locations in Asia, including Taiwan, Korea, Japan, Malaysia, Indonesia, Thailand and Hong Kong, and in Amsterdam, Budapest, Glasgow, Paris, Rome in Europe.

The meetings usually started with a review of demand, production, sales and capacity in the main sales areas, including Europe, then prices were discussed, including for individual customers, i.e.  TV and computer manufacturers, said the EC.
''These cartels for cathode ray  tubes are 'textbook cartels': they feature all the worst kinds of
anti-competitive behaviour that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe," EU
Competition Commissioner Joaquin Almunia said in a statement.

He said the violations affected consumers, as cathode ray  tubes accounted for 50 to 70 per cent of the price of a screen.

Philips was fined 313.4 million, LG Electronics 295.6 million Panasonic 157.5 million, Samsung SDI
150.8 million, Toshiba 28 million and Technicolor 38.6 million. Two Panasonic joint ventures were
also fined a total of 94 million.

Philips, LG Electronics, Samsung SDI and three other firms were fined a record 1.47 billion euros by EU
antitrust regulators for  fixing  prices of TV and monitor cathode-ray tubes for nearly a decade.

TD

"These cartels for cathode ray tubes are 'textbook cartels': they feature all the worst kinds of anti-competitive behaviour that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe," EU Competition Commissioner Joaquin Almunia said in a statement. HD     EC lashes Samsung, Panasonic  and other CRT makers with €1.47bn price fixing fine
BY     Lawrence Latif

SC     VNUN

The European Commission (EC) has fined several firms including Samsung, Panasonic, Philips and LG
€1.47bn for fixing  the prices of cathode ray tubes (CRTs).

The EC levied the  fines against most of the biggest names in the CRT business for participating in one or two cartels between 1996 and 2006.

TD
The EC's €1.47bn  fine was handed down after it said the firms had "fixed prices, shared markets, allocated customers between themselves and restricted their output" of CRT units, with the commission claiming that the cartels were "among the most organised cartels" it has investigated.

With Taiwanese hardware manufacturer Chunghwa blowing the whistle and escaping the  fine, it was left to the others to carry the can, though other firms involved got partial reductions. LG Electronics and Philips came out the worst, both getting hit with two  fines with LG getting one for €295m and Philips
€313m, while the two received a separate joint  fine of €391m.

Samsung and Panasonic got hit with €150m and €157m respectively, while two other Panasonic joint ventures were hit with €86m and €7m  fines. Toshiba and Technicolor received the smallest  fines, €38m and €28m respectively.
"These cartels for cathode ray  tubes are 'textbook cartels': they feature all the worst kinds of
anti-competitive behaviour that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe," said
Joaquín Almunia, the Commission's vice president in charge of competition policy.

"Cathode ray  tubes were a very important component in the making of  television and computer screens. They accounted for 50 to 70 [percent] of the price of a screen. This gives an indication of the serious harm this illegal behaviour has caused both to  television and computer screen producers in the EEA
[European Economic Area], and ultimately the harm it caused to the European consumers over the years."

The EC finally managed to bring to book a number of CRT manufacturers for price  fixing  more than five years after the offences, by which time CRTs have been largely superceded.

HD       EU slaps record $1.9B fine on cartel
BY       By Mark Thompson
WC      358 words
PD       5 December 2012
ET       06:01 PM SN       CNN Wire SC       CNNWR LA       English
CY       Copyright 2012 Cable News Network. All Rights Reserved.

LP
LONDON (CNNMoney) -- Six companies who fixed the markets for  television and computer screen
tubes over a 10-year period have been hit with a record  fine of 1.47 billion euros.

The European Commission said the companies were fully aware that their attempts to fix prices, share markets and coordinate production of cathode ray  tubes between 1996 and 2006 were illegal.

TD
Philips, LG Electronics, Samsung SDI, Panasonic, Technicolor (formerly Thomson of France), Toshiba and Taiwan's Chungwha were all guilty of operating "textbook cartels," EU Competition Commissioner Joaquin Almunia said in a statement.

"They feature all the worst kinds of anti-competitive behavior that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe," Almunia said.
Dutch-based Philips was hit with the biggest individual  fine of 313.4 million euros, while South Korea's
LG Electronics received a penalty of 295.6 million. Philips and LG were also jointly liable for a  fine worth
392 million euros.
Chungwa escaped punishment because it blew the whistle on the  cartel.

Cathode-ray tubes have largely been replaced by liquid-crystal display (LCD), plasma technology and light-emitting diodes (LED). But they used to account for up to 70% of the price of a screen.

The commission said the  cartel was trying to limit the impact of the decline in the industry at the expense of consumers.
It also published details showing the sophistication of the  cartel's operations.

Top managers from the firms would set the high-level framework at "green meetings" -- so called because they were often followed by a game of golf -- while detailed implementation would be agreed upon by junior executives who sometimes met as often as every week in Asia and Europe.

Plant visits would be carried out to ensure compliance with agreed limits on capacity, and some papers discovered during the anti-trust investigation carried the instructions: "Please dispose of the following document after reading it."

The previous record for a European anti-trust  fine was 1.38 billion euros, imposed by the  European       Commission on a car glass cartel in 2008.

SE               OnLine
HD               EU imposes  biggest  ever cartel fine on screen producers including Philips,  LG, Panasonic
BY               Raf Casert, The Associated Press
WC              326 words
PD               5 December 2012
SN               Postmedia Breaking News
SC               CWNS
LA                English
CY               Copyright © 2012 Canwest News Service

LP
BRUSSELS - The European Union on Wednesday imposed its biggest ever cartel fine of almost
€1.47 billion ($1.96 billion) on seven companies for  fixing  the market of television and computer
monitor tubes.

The EU's Commission ruled that, for a decade ending in 2006, the companies - including Philips, LG Electronics and Panasonic - artificially set prices, shared markets and restricted their output at the expense of the consumer.

TD
EU antitrust Commissioner Joaquin Almunia said that the companies' actions "feature all the worst kinds of anticompetitive behaviour that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe."

Tubes were the essential part of television screens and computer monitors before they were replaced by flatscreens. The cathode ray tubes accounted for up to 70 per cent of the cost of a screen, the Commission said.

Alumina added that the  tubes' cost gave "an indication of the serious harm" the companies had caused.

Philips and LG Electronics, which acted jointly and separately, were fined a combined €999 million ($1.3 billion) with Panasonic punished with a €157 million ($205 million) fine, adding to more if combined fines and affiliates were included.

Other companies  fined were Samsung SDI, Technicolor, MTPD and Toshiba. Chunghwa of Taiwan escaped fines as it was the first to reveal the  cartel to the EU.
"It is the biggest  fine for a cartel, ever," Almunia said. The  fine exceeded the previous record of the
2008 fine of €1.38 billion ($1.8 billion) in a car glass  cartel.

Almunia said the  tubes cartel operated worldwide with the companies involved fully realizing they acted illegally.

One document seized by the Commission had a warning "Everybody is requested to keep it as secret as it would be serious damage if it is open to customers or  European Commission".
Another documents said "Please dispose the following document after reading it".

HD         EU slaps biggest-ever fine on screen producers
WC         273 words
PD          5 December 2012
ET          03:28 PM
SN          Deutsche Welle
SC          DEUEN
LA          English
CY          (c) 2012. Deutsche Welle.

LP
EU anti-trust regulators have  fined six major electronics firms a record total penalty of 1.4 billion euros ($1.9 billion). The companies were deemed to have fixed prices for  TV and monitor tubes for nearly a decade.

The largest  fine, totaling 313 million euros ($409 million), was slapped on Dutch electronics firm Philips, the European Union's executive Commission said Wednesday, ahead of a 295-million-euro penalty imposed on LG Electronics, and 157 million for Panasonic.

TD
The other electronics giants involved in the international price  fixing  were Samsung, Toshiba and
Technicolor.

According to EU Competition Commissioner Joaquin Almunia, the firms fixed global prices for so-called cathode ray tubes. These tubes used to be key components of  television screens and computer monitors until flatscreens have started to replace them in recent years.
The six companies formed altogether two cartels, one involving  TV sets and the other  computer
monitors, and had been operating on a global scale between 1996 and 2006.

"These cartels feature all the worst kinds of anti-competitive behavior that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe," Almunia said in a statement, adding that the violations were especially harmful to consumers as the  tubes accounted for up to 70 percent of screen prices.

Taiwanese firm Chunghwa Picture  Tubes finally blew the whistle on the cartels and escaped being punished, the  EU Commission said.
Before Wednesday's ruling, the biggest fine imposed for anti-trust violations by the EU amounted to
1.38 billion euros slapped on a car glass cartel in 2008.
uhe/msh (Reuters, AP, dpa)

TD
Electronics giants Philips Electronics N.V ., Samsung SDI Co. Ltd. and Panasonic Corp. are among seven companies fined EUR1.47 billion ($1.92 billion) by European regulators Wednesday for operating cartels in television- and computer-monitor tubes over two decades.

The European Commission said LG Electronics Inc. along with Philips and Samsung participated in both cartels, while Panasonic, Toshiba Corp., MTP -- a Panasonic subsidiary -- and Technicolor, formerly Thomson, participated in the  cartel for television tubes.

HD            WSJ UPDATE: EU Fines Seven for TV, Monitor-Tubes Cartel
WC            795 words
PD             5 December 2012
ET             04:53 PM
SN             Dow Jones News Service
SC             DJ
LA             English
CY             (c) 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

LP
(Adds details)



By Vanessa Mock

TD
BRUSSELS--Global electronics giants Philips Electronics NV (PHIA.AE), Samsung SDI Co. Ltd. (006400.SE) and Panasonic Corp. (PC) were among seven companies fined a record 1.47 billion euros ($1.92 billion) by European regulators Wednesday for operating price-fixing  cartels in TV and computer parts.

The firms were found guilty of involvement in one or two separate cartels set up to fix the market for cathode ray tubes, the main component in color  television and computer screens but now largely defunct as a result of the advent of plasma and LCD screens.

The fine is the highest ever that the  European Commission has handed down for forming cartels. The cartels began in 1996 and ended 10 years later. The EU and other antitrust regulators have been investigating the issue for the past three years.

"These cartels for cathode ray  tubes are 'textbook cartels': They feature all the worst kinds of anticompetitive behavior that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe," said Joaquin Almunia, the European Union's antitrust chief.

Regulators said the firms conspired in a gamut of harmful practices, from price  fixing  to coordinating capacity and output of their products. At their peak, cathode ray  tubes accounted for up to 70% of the price of a TV or computer screen.

Dutch electronics firm Philips received the highest fine, EUR313 million, for participating in its own right in both the  TV- and computer-monitor tube cartel, and EUR392 million shared with LG Electronics for a joint venture. LG Electronics Inc. (066570.SE) must pay EUR296 million for collusion in its own right, and Samsung SDI EUR151 million. The two South Korean firms were also found
guilty of joining both cartels.

Philips Chief Executive Frans van Houten said in a statement he regretted "any association with this type of behavior."
"Our ethical standards. are very clear and must be strictly adhered to," he said.

Nevertheless, the  fine, which relates to a business divested in 2001, was "disproportionate and unjustified," he said.

Panasonic said the  European Commission's decision was "factually and legally erroneous," and it would "carefully review the decision and consider our options for appeal to the European courts."

LG Electronics said it would provide a statement once the company has reviewed the  European       Commission's decision in detail.


Page 62 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
A spokesman for Samsung SDI said it would reserve comment until confirming details of the decision with European regulators.
Panasonic, Toshiba Corp. (6502.TO), MTPD--a Panasonic subsidiary--and Technicolor
(TCH.FR)--formerly Thomson--participated in the  cartel for television tubes. Panasonic and Toshiba
were fined EUR157 million and EUR28 million respectively. MTPD will pay part of a EUR95 million
fine, shared with Panasonic and Toshiba. Spokespeople from Panasonic and Toshiba weren't
immediately available for comment. Technicolor said in a statement on its website that Thomson sold
its cathode ray tube business in 2005.

Because it revealed the  cartel's existence, whistleblower Chunghwa of Taiwan received full immunity from a fine that otherwise would have been EUR17 million. Philips and Samsung received reductions of their fines for cooperating in the probe.

The commission said the two cartels were among the "most organized" it had investigated, and said the firms had been well aware that they were breaking the law.

Mr. Almunia said senior executives referred to their gatherings in Europe and Asia as "green meetings," because they were regularly followed by a golf game. The deals cut during those encounters were then implemented during more regular, lower-level "glass meetings", which Mr. Almunia described as the "engine room of the  cartel," held in a string of locations ranging from Paris to Hong Kong.

In one document uncovered during the probe, one  cartel member boasted that "because of the success of the glass meeting, everybody has been enjoying business this year." Another urged other members to "keep it a secret as it would be serious damage if it is open to customers and  European Commission."

Demand for cathode  tubes was booming at the start of the  cartel, while in 2003, when the technology was replaced by LCD,  cartel members were trying to manage the decline in the market, Mr. Almunia told reporters, adding that the firms may even have "artificially slowed" the transition to plasma.

In setting the  fines, the commission said it took into account the companies' sales as well as the geographic scope, severity and duration of the  cartel.

Other regulators around the globe, including in the U.S., are also still investigating the cartels and have yet to make their decisions.
