CLM     Top Stories
HD       EU Fines Seven for TV, PC Tubes Cartel
WC       778 words
PD        5 December 2012
ET        12:41 PM
SN        Dow Jones Top Global Market Stories
SC        DJTGMS
LA        English
CY        Copyright © 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

LP
By Vanessa Mock

BRUSSELS--Global electronics giants Philips Electronics NV (), Samsung SDI Co. Ltd. (006400.SE) and Panasonic Corp. (PC) were among seven companies fined a record 1.47 billion euros ($1.92 billion) by European regulators Wednesday for operating price-fixing  cartels in TV and computer parts.

TD
The firms were found guilty of involvement in one or two separate cartels set up to fix the market for cathode ray tubes, the main component in color  television and computer screens but now largely defunct as a result of the advent of plasma and LCD screens.

The fine is the highest ever that the  European Commission has handed down for forming cartels. The cartels began in 1996 and ended 10 years later. The EU and other antitrust regulators have been investigating the issue for the past three years.

"These cartels for cathode ray  tubes are "textbook cartels': They feature all the worst kinds of anticompetitive behavior that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe," said Joaquin Almunia, the European Union's antitrust chief.

Regulators said the firms conspired in a gamut of harmful practices, from price  fixing  to coordinating capacity and output of their products. At their peak, cathode ray  tubes accounted for up to 70% of the price of a TV or computer screen.

Dutch electronics firm Philips received the highest  fine, EUR313 million, for participating in its own right in both the  TV- and computer-monitor tube  cartel, and EUR392 million shared with LG Electronics for a joint venture. LG Electronics Inc. (066570.SE) must pay EUR296 million for collusion in its own right,
and Samsung SDI EUR151 million. The two South Korean firms were also found guilty of joining both cartels.

Philips Chief Executive Frans van Houten said in a statement he regretted "any association with this type of behavior."
"Our ethical standards. are very clear and must be strictly adhered to," he said.

Nevertheless, the  fine, which relates to a business divested in 2001, was "disproportionate and unjustified," he said.

Panasonic said the  European Commission's decision was "factually and legally erroneous," and it would "carefully review the decision and consider our options for appeal to the European courts."

LG Electronics said it would provide a statement once the company has reviewed the  European            Commission's decision in detail.

A spokesman for Samsung SDI said it would reserve comment until confirming details of the decision with European regulators.


Page 71 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Panasonic, Toshiba Corp. (6502.TO), MTPD--a Panasonic subsidiary--and Technicolor
(TCH.FR)--formerly Thomson--participated in the  cartel for television tubes. Panasonic and Toshiba
were fined EUR157 million and EUR28 million respectively. MTPD will pay part of a EUR95 million  fine,
shared with Panasonic and Toshiba. Spokespeople from Panasonic and Toshiba weren't immediately
available for comment. Technicolor said in a statement on its website that Thomson sold its cathode ray
tube business in 2005.

Because it revealed the  cartel's existence, whistleblower Chunghwa of Taiwan received full immunity from a fine that otherwise would have been EUR17 million. Philips and Samsung received reductions of their fines for cooperating in the probe.

The commission said the two cartels were among the "most organized" it had investigated, and said the firms had been well aware that they were breaking the law.

Mr. Almunia said senior executives referred to their gatherings in Europe and Asia as "green meetings," because they were regularly followed by a golf game. The deals cut during those encounters were then implemented during more regular, lower-level "glass meetings', which Mr. Almunia described as the "engine room of the  cartel," held in a string of locations ranging from Paris to Hong Kong.

In one document uncovered during the probe, one  cartel member boasted that "because of the success of the glass meeting, everybody has been enjoying business this year." Another urged other members to "keep it a secret as it would be serious damage if it is open to customers and  European Commission."

Demand for cathode tubes was booming at the start of the  cartel, while in 2003, when the technology was replaced by LCD, cartel members were trying to manage the decline in the market, Mr. Almunia told reporters, adding that the firms may even have "artificially slowed" the transition to plasma.

In setting the  fines, the commission said it took into account the companies' sales as well as the geographic scope, severity and duration of the  cartel.

Other regulators around the globe, including in the U.S., are also still investigating the cartels and have yet to make their decisions.
Write to Vanessa Mock at vanessa.mock@dowjones.com

SE              Consumer Goods
HD              EC levies €1.5bn fine on TV tube cartel
BY              Alex Spence
WC             542 words
PD              5 December 2012
ET              03:03 PM
SN              thetimes.co.uk
SC              TIMEUK
LA              English
CY              © 2012 Times Newspapers Ltd. All rights reserved

LP
A group of the world’s leading electronics manufacturers have been fined almost €1.5 billion (£1.21 billion) for rigging the market for components used in televisions and computer monitors.

LG, Samsung, Philips, Panasonic and Toshiba were among seven companies hit with penalties of up to €314 million each by Europe’s top antitrust watchdog for operating a decade-long  cartel that distorted markets and harmed consumers.

TD
Collectively the penalty is the biggest the  European Commission has imposed in a cartel case.

The enforcement action could leave the electronics giants facing the threat of follow-on legal action by groups of consumers who were overcharged as a result of the collusion.

The Commission said there were two cartels, one relating to the sale of colour picture  tubes used in televisions and the other to  computer monitors, which together were among the most organised the competition regulator has ever investigated.

“For almost ten years, the cartelists carried out the most harmful anti-competitive practices including price fixing,  market sharing, customer allocation, capacity and output co-ordination and exchanges of commercially sensitive information," the Commission said.
Executives from the companies conspired to rig the markets at clandestine meetings nicknamed
“green meetings" because they were often followed by a round of golf, the regulator said.

The exact implementation of the anti-competitive schemes were left to their lieutenants to work out at other meetings in cities such as Hong Kong, Amsterdam, Paris and Rome.
The cartels operated worldwide, the Commission said.

The companies were aware they were breaking the law, as revealed in one document uncovered by the investigation that warned: “Everybody is requested to keep it secret as it would be serious damage if it is open to customers or the  European Commission."

Another document discovered by the Commission came with an instruction to “Please dispose the following document after reading it".

The biggest  fines were against Philips and LG, which were involved in both cartels. They received penalties of €313.4 million and €295.6 million respectively.
Samsung, which was also involved in both cartels, was  fined €150.8 million.

Panasonic and Toshiba, which only participated in the  television tubes cartel, were hit with penalties of €157.5 million and €28 million respectively.
Other companies received smaller  fines, bringing the grand total to €1,470,515.

Page 75 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Chunghwa, a Taiwanese manufacturer, received immunity because it blew the whistle on the price
fixing.  It otherwise would have received  fines of close to €17 million, the Commission said.
The Commission’s investigation began with a series of raids in November 2007.

Joaquin Almunia, Europe’s Competition Commissioner, said: “These cartels for cathode ray  tubes are textbook cartels. They feature all the worst kinds of anticompetitive behaviour that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe.

“Cathode ray tubes were a very important component in the making of  television and computer screens. They accounted for 50 to 70 per cent of the price of a screen. This gives an indication of the serious harm this illegal behaviour has caused both to  television and computer screen producers in the EEA, and ultimately the harm it caused to the European consumers over the years."

THE HAGUE, Dec. 5 (Xinhua) -- Dutch electronics company Philips will appeal against a
313-million-euro (410 million U.S. dollars)  fine they received from the  European Commission, the
company announced on Wednesday.

In total, the Commission  fined seven companies such as Philips, LG Electronics and Samsung a record 1.47 billion euros on Wednesday for  fixing  prices of TV cathode-ray tubes in two cartels for a period of almost ten years.

TD
"These cartels for cathode ray  tubes are 'textbook cartels.' They feature all the worst kinds of anticompetitive behavior that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe," EU Competition Commissioner Joaquin Almunia said.

Philips said the  fine was "disproportional and injust." "We regret to be associated with this kind of behavior," spokesperson Joost Akkermans told Xinhua.

BRUSSELS, Dec. 5 (Yonhap) -- South Korea's LG Electronics and Samsung SDI are among the companies fined a record total of 1.47 billion euros (US$1.9 billion) by the European Union antitrust regulator over price-rigging of  television cathode ray tubes, the authorities said Wednesday.

The European CommissionfinedLG Electronics 295.6 million euros and Samsung SDI 150.8 million euros, for fixing  prices of now-obsolete cathode ray  tubes with five other international firms over roughly a decade.

TD
The watchdog imposed the biggest penalty on Dutch-based Philips with 313.4 million euros.

The firms illegally "fixed prices, shared markets, allocated customers between themselves and restricted their output" from 1996 until 2006 in the markets for  television and computer cathode ray tubes, the commission said in a statement.
The tubes accounted for 50-70 percent of the price of screens.

The regulator said they acted as "textbook cartels" by featuring "all the worst kinds of anticompetitive behavior that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe."
The commission raided the companies in 2007. ejkim@yna.co.kr (END)

BRUSSELS, Dec. 5 (Yonhap) -- South Korea's LG Electronics and Samsung SDI are among the companies fined a record total of 1.47 billion euros (US$1.9 billion) by the European Union antitrust regulator over price-rigging of  television cathode ray tubes, authorities said Wednesday.

TD
The European CommissionfinedLG Electronics 295.6 million euros and Samsung SDI 150.8 million euros, for fixing  prices of now-obsolete cathode ray  tubes with five other international firms over roughly a decade.

restricted their output" from 1996 until 2006 in the markets for  television and computer cathode ray
tubes, the commission said in a statement.
The tubes accounted for 50-70 percent of the price of screens.

The regulator said they acted as "textbook cartels" by featuring "all the worst kinds of anticompetitive behavior that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe."
The commission raided the companies in 2007.

In a statement released in Seoul, LG Electronics said it plans to decide measures to take following a close review of the commission's report.

HD              EU FinesPanasonic, Toshiba, Others for Tube Cartels

Brussels, Dec. 5 (Jiji Press)--The  European Commission said Wednesday that it has finedPanasonic Corp. <6752>, Toshiba Corp. <6502> and four other major electronics makers for fixing  prices on television and computer monitor tubes.

The fines on the companies totaled 1.47 billion euros, a record for any  cartel case handled by the executive arm of the European Union.

TD
The fines include 157 million euros on Panasonic and 28 million euros on Toshiba, both Japanese manufacturers.
The four others are South Korean makers LG Electronics Inc. and Samsung SDI Co. and Royal
Philips Electronics of the Netherlands and France's Technicolor.

In addition, Panasonic, Toshiba and MT Picture Display Co. were together  fined 95 million euros. MT Picture was once a joint venture between Panasonic and Toshiba before Panasonic took full control of it.
Taiwan's Chunghwa Picture Tube Ltd. was also involved in the price-fixing  but exempted from a
fine because it was the first to notify the commission of the cartels.

The commission said the firms fixed prices, shared markets, allocated customers between themselves and restricted their output between 1996 and 2006.
END

HD        LG Electronics: Will Appeal European Commission's Decision  on Alleged Participation in Cartel
BY        By Min-Jeong Lee
WC       336 words
PD        6 December 2012
ET        12:21 AM
SN        Dow Jones Global Equities News
SC        DJI
LA         English
CY        (c) 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

LP

SEOUL--South Korean consumer-electronics maker LG Electronics Inc. (066570.SE) said Thursday it will appeal the European Commission's decision to  fine the company for its alleged participation in price-fixing  cartels in television and computer parts.

TD
LG Electronics was among seven companies that were fined a record 1.47 billion euros ($1.92 billion) by European regulators Wednesday for operating price-fixing  cartels for cathode ray  tubes, the main component in color TV and computer screens before the advent of plasma and liquid crystal display screens.

The South Korean company said in a press release the  European Commission had fined it a total 492 million euros for allegedly infringing European competition law relating to the sale of the parts starting in the late 1990s and lasting until 2006.
It added it disagrees with the  fine, which comprises a 296 million euro penalty on LG alone and a further
392 million euro fine that the South Korean company must share with Philips Electronics NV for a 50-50
joint venture the two companies set up in 2001 called LG Philips Displays.

"Other leading competition authorities, including the Korean Fair Trade Commission, the U.S. Department of Justice and the Canadian Competition Bureau have investigated the same facts and concluded that LG Electronics should not be held liable for the conduct of LG Philips Displays," John Kwon, executive vice president of LG Electronics, said in the release.

He added the company can't understand why the  European Commission has taken a wholly different approach compared with other authorities.

The European Commission also fined South Korean display maker Samsung SDI Co. (006400.SE). A spokesman for the company said Thursday Samsung SDI has yet to decide on whether to appeal the European Commission's decision.
Write to Min-Jeong Lee at min-jeong.lee@dowjones.com

Alleged Participation in  Cartel

TD


By Min-Jeong Lee

SEOUL--South Korean consumer-electronics maker LG Electronics Inc. (066570.SE) said Thursday it will appeal the European Commission's decision to  fine the company for its alleged participation in price-fixing  cartels in television and computer parts.

LG Electronics was among seven companies that were fined a record 1.47 billion euros ($1.92 billion) by European regulators Wednesday for operating price-fixing  cartels for cathode ray  tubes, the main component in color TV and computer screens before the advent of plasma and liquid crystal display screens.

The South Korean company said in a press release the  European Commission had fined it a total 492 million euros for allegedly infringing European competition law relating to the sale of the parts starting in the late 1990s and lasting until 2006.

It added it disagrees with the  fine, which comprises a 296 million euro penalty on LG alone and a further 392 million euro  fine that the South Korean company must share with Philips Electronics NV for a 50-50 joint venture the two companies set up in 2001 called LG Philips Displays.

"Other leading competition authorities, including the Korean Fair Trade Commission, the U.S. Department of Justice and the Canadian Competition Bureau have investigated the same facts and concluded that LG Electronics should not be held liable for the conduct of LG Philips Displays," John Kwon, executive vice president of LG Electronics, said in the release.

He added the company can't understand why the  European Commission has taken a wholly different approach compared with other authorities.

The European Commission also fined South Korean display maker Samsung SDI Co. (006400.SE). A spokesman for the company said Thursday Samsung SDI has yet to decide on whether to appeal the European Commission's decision.

SE           Business
HD           EU fines seven firms a record £1.2bn for TV tube cartel
BY           Bruno Waterfield
WC          353 words
PD           6 December 2012
SN           The Daily Telegraph
SC           DT
ED           1; National
PG           9
LA           English

LP
THE European Union has imposed its biggest ever cartel fine of €1.5bn (£1.2bn) on seven companies caught rigging the market for the cathode–ray tubes used to make television and computer
monitors.

Between 1996 and 2006, the companies – including Philips, LG Electronics and Panasonic – were found to have artifi–cially fixed prices, shared markets and restricted their output at the expense of the consumer.

TD
"It is the biggest  fine for a cartel, ever," said Joaquin Almunia, the EU's competition commissioner and vice–president of the European Commission.

Mr Almunia described the case as featuring "all the worst kinds of anti–competitive behaviour that are strictly forbidden" under EU law.
Philips and LG Electronics, which acted jointly and separately, were  fined a combined €999m, while
Panasonic was punished with a €157m  fine.

Samsung SDI, Technicolor, MTPD and Toshiba were also  fined, but Chunghwa of Taiwan escaped fines, as it was the first to reveal the  cartel to the authorities. The total penalties for the  cartel exceeded the previous record  fine of €1.38bn levied in a car glass market–fixing  case, four years ago. Commission investigators found that the  cartel operated worldwide, with all the companies involved fully aware that they were acting illegally.

One document seized by officials during a raid carried a warning that revealed the deliberate wrongdoing. "Everybody is requested to keep it a secret, as it would be serious damage if it is open to customers or European Commission," said the document.

Top management–level meetings were dubbed "green meetings" by the  cartel companies because they were often followed by a golf game.

Preparation and implementation of the  cartel was carried out at lower–level meetings, often referred to as "glass meetings", on a quarterly, monthly or sometimes even weekly basis.
A statement from Philips said the company intended to appeal against the decision and insisted it had
"fully co–operated" with the investigation.

HD     EU imposes  record fine on LG, Samsung and four others
WC     314 words
PD      6 December 2012
SN      ecPulse SC      ECPULS LA      English

LP
The European Commission imposed a record fine on Samsung, Panasonic, Philips, LG Electronics, Toshiba and Technicolor worth 1.47 billion euros ($1.92 billion) for running two cartels for nearly a decade. According to the  European Commission, executives from the above mentioned companies met until six years ago to fix prices and divide up markets for  TV and computer monitor cathode-ray  tubes. "These cartels for cathode-ray  tubes are `textbook cartels`: they feature all the worst kinds of
anti-competitive behavior that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe", EU Competition Commissioner Joaquin Almunia said. The Dutch-based firm Philips received the biggest penalty. It was fined 313.4 million euros and faces a further penalty through a joint venture due to its role in the price fixing  and dividing up the markets.

TD
The South Korean firm LG Electronics was  fined 295.6 million euros and must pay its share of a joint venture penalty, while the Japanese firm Panasonic was  fined 157.5 million euros. Meanwhile the South Korean firm Samsung SDI was  fined 150.8 million euros, the Japanese firm Toshiba Corp. was  fined 28 million euros while the French company Technicolor was  fined 38.6 million euros. Joaquin Almunia also said that these violations were mainly harmful for consumers, as cathode-ray  tubes were a very important component in the making of  television and computer screens, accounting for 50%-70% of the price of a screen. This technology however is out of date for flat screens that now use more advanced display technologies like the liquid-crystal display (LCD), plasma display and organic light-emitting diodes. Although the  fine is significant, it was expected. The Commission`s biggest antitrust penalty until now was of 1.38 billion euros imposed on participants in a car glass  cartel in 2008.

SE         COMPANIES
HD         EU in record €1.47bn cartel fine; ELECTRONIC EQUIPMENT BY         By Alex Barker in Brussels and Simon Mundy in Seoul
WC        379 words

CY         Copyright 2012 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web.

LP
Brussels acts over cathode tube market
LG, Samsung SDI and Philips involved

TD
Philips, LG Electronics and Samsung SDI were hit with a record €1.47bn  fine from Europe's top competition enforcer yesterday after participating in "textbook cartels" for almost a decade.
Seven electronics groups were punished for two conspiracies to rig prices and supply of cathode ray
tubes.

European Commission investigators found that managers at "greens meetings" plotted to carve up markets and overcharge consumers often before playing golf at clubs in Singapore, Korea and Japan. One included the Palm Garden golf club in Malaysia.

Staff then developed the details of the arrangements at sometimes weekly "glass meetings" - named after the material covering the  tubes - where information was shared and prices set, sometimes for individual TV and computer manufacturers.

The combined €1.5bn penalty for the two cartels is the biggest  fine imposed by the European               Commission in a single decision and exceeds the €1.4bn levied on a  cartel on glass for car windows.

Joaquín Almunia, the EU's competition chief, said the two conspiracies featured the "worst kinds of anti-competitive behaviour" that caused "serious harm" to  TV and computer makers.
The price-fixing,  market-sharing and customer allocation became almost routine between 1996 and
2006.

While the collusion began at the peak of the market for cathode  tubes, it became a way to mitigate the drop-off in sales as customers moved to slim display screens using plasma or liquid crystal.

In one document seized by investigators, a  cartel member describes setting a goal to "control supply and maintain a proper price to survive". Another asks for the arrangements to be kept "secret". "Please dispose the following document after reading it," it said.
Mr Almunia said the high  fine reflected the long duration of the cartels and the breadth of co-ordination. Chunghwa of Taiwan won immunity for alerting authorities to the wrongdoing. LG Electronics, Philips
and Samsung SDI, an arm of the Korean conglomerate, participated in both cartels. Panasonic,
Toshiba, MTPD (now a Panasonic subsidiary) and Technicolor only colluded on  TV parts.
Philips faces the biggest penalty of €450m. It said it would appeal.

News | EUROPEAN COMMISSION

Executives from some of the world's biggest electronics manufacturers met in Europe and Asia to illegally fix the price of televisions and computers for a decade, the  European Commission (EC) ruled yesterday.

TD
Handing out £1.1bn  fines to Philips, Panasonic, Samsung and four other firms, Brussels ruled that between 1996 and 2006 they held back supply of cathode ray  tubes, then the key component in television sets and computer monitors.

Because the  tubes made up between 50 and 70 per cent of the retail price, consumers paid an "over price" for TVs and computers. The scam was "among the most organised cartels that the Commission has investigated", the EC said.

The cartelists' "anti-competitive practices" included price  fixing,  market sharing, customer allocation and exchanging commercially-sensitive information.
Philips said it would challenge what it called a disproportionate and unjustified penalty.

HD            Electronics firms fined £1.1bn for fixing  prices of TVs and computers
BY            By MARTIN HICKMAN Consumer Affairs Correspondent

LP
News

Executives from some of the world's biggest electronics manufacturers met in Europe and Asia to illegally fix the price of televisions and computers for a decade, the  European Commission (EC) ruled yesterday.

TD
Handing out huge  fines to Philips, Panasonic, Samsung and four other firms, Brussels ruled that between 1996 and 2006 they held back supply of cathode ray  tubes, then the key component in  TV sets and computer monitors.

Because the  tubes made up between 50 and 70 per cent of the retail price, consumers consequently paid an "over price" for TVs and computers. The scam was "among the most organised cartels that the Commission has investigated", the EC said.

The cartelists' "anti-competitive practices" included price  fixing,  market sharing, customer allocation and exchanging commercially-sensitive information.

Staff were told to destroy incriminating documents. Executives would agree the frauds at "green" meetings - so-called because afterwards they would play golf.

"For almost 10 years… these companies fixed prices, shared markets, allocated customers between themselves and restricted their output," said the EC, which  fined the seven a total of €1.4bn (£1.1bn).

Some of the best-known names in electronics - Philips, Samsung SDI, LG Electronics, Panasonic, Toshiba, MTPD, Technicolor and Chunghwa Picture  Tubes - ran different cartels for  television tubes and computer tubes.
Philips said it would challenge what it called a disproportionate and unjustified penalty. Panasonic and
Toshiba are also considering legal challenges. Samsung reserved its comment.

SE       finance
HD       E.U.’s heftiest  price-fixing fine levied on picture-tube cartel; 7 technology groups  in Europe and
Asia are penalized €1.5 billion
BY       By JAMES KANTER
WC      942 words
PD       6 December 2012
SN       International Herald Tribune
SC       INHT
PG       18
LA        English
CY       © 2012 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP
The conspirators called them ‘‘greens meetings,’’ because they took place on golf courses.
Senior managers at some of the world’s largest electronics companies often used those meetings,
mostly in Asia, to fix the price of picture and display  tubes for televisions and  computer screens, the top
European antitrust regulator said Wednesday.

TD
Joaquín Almunia, the E.U. competition commissioner, said he would levy  fines totaling almost €1.5
billion, or $1.96 billion, on seven companies involved in the two cartels, which operated for a decade until
2006. Combined, the  fines amount to the largest single penalty for price  fixing  ever imposed by the
commission.

The action follows a spate of similar cases in the glass and display sectors, where bulky cathode ray tubes have been supplanted by technologies like liquid crystal display and plasma that allow manufacturers to build far more compact monitors and screens.

Mr. Almunia imposed the strongest penalties on Philips Electronics of the Netherlands and LG Electronics of South Korea.

Mr. Almunia said at a news conference that the  cartel activity began in the late 1990s, when the market was still strong for cathode ray  tubes, and lasted until 2006 even as that market declined, allowing the conspirators to continue generating strong returns for a technology that was rapidly becoming outmoded.

‘‘The companies were trying to manage through collusion the decline in the market for these kinds of tubes,’’ Mr. Almunia said. ‘‘The undue profits that the companies derived from the collusion may even have artificially slowed down the transition to the more modern products like LCD and plasma displays.’’

Excerpts of minutes from meetings held by the  cartel members obtained during the investigation showed the efforts they made to stem the tide of the newer products, according to commission officials.

‘‘Producers need to avoid price competition through controlling their production capacity (of flat types in particular),’’ one excerpt read. Another noted that ‘‘mutual cooperation is required to deal with an expected economic downturn’’ in the second half of 2002.

In addition to the ‘‘greens meetings,’’ there were ‘‘glass meetings’’ for lower-level managers, the name probably related to the glass structure of the cathode ray  tubes, officials said. They were held in Asia and in European cities including Glasgow, Paris, Rome, Amsterdam and Budapest, commission officials said.

The cartels ‘‘feature all the worst kinds of anti-competitive behavior that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe,’’ Mr. Almunia said. There had been ‘‘serious harm’’ to producers in Europe and to consumers, he said, since the cathode ray  tubes had accounted for up to 70 percent of the price of screens.


Page 94 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
The commission’s antitrust division can  fine offenders up to 10 percent of their annual worldwide sales, and the fine on Wednesday exceeded the previous record of almost €1.4 billion, which was imposed in
2008, for a car-glass  cartel.

But unlike regulators in the United States, the commission has no criminal enforcement powers and cannot prosecute or seek to jail participants for anti-competitive offenses. Many lawyers say that remains a shortcoming of the European system.

‘‘There is a deterrent effect as these  fines get higher, but there are always going to be some companies with wayward commercial personnel and some companies where some people say, ‘Let’s take the risk as might be worth it in the long run,’’’ said Caroline Hobson, a competition law partner at CMS Cameron McKenna in London. ‘‘An even greater enforcement power is the power to jail offenders because that really makes people wake up.’’
She said she did not represent any of the companies involved in the case.

The sanctions announced Wednesday were the latest imposed by the commission on activity in the sector. It levied a fine of €128.7 million last year against four producers of the glass that is used in cathode ray tubes, and in 2010 fined members of a cartel that fixed prices on flat-panel displays based on LCD technology €649 million. One of the companies involved in that case, Chunghwa from Taiwan, reported the picture  tubes case to the commission. As a result, Chunghwa received full immunity from fines.

On Wednesday, the commission  fined Philips the largest amount, €313.4 million. LG Electronics was fined €295.6 million. Philips and LG also were also part of a joint venture that received an additional  fine of €391.9 million for which both companies were liable.

In a statement, Philips said it would appeal the  fines to the General Court of the European Union, the bloc’s second-highest tribunal. Philips described the  fine relating to its involvement in the joint venture as
‘‘disproportionate and unjustified.’’ Philips said it had divested that business in 2001.
LG did not respond to an e-mail request for comment.
The commission also finedPanasonic €157.5 million, Samsung €150.8 million, Toshiba €28 million and
Technicolor €38.6 million.

The commission said the cartels operated for a decade, beginning around 1996, and had engaged in the most organized market manipulation it had ever investigated. The conspiracies included price  fixing, market sharing, customer allocation and exchanges of sensitive commercial information.
During his news conference, Mr. Almunia read from one of the documents obtained by the commission
to show that  cartel members were aware they were breaking the law. ‘‘Everybody is requested to keep it
a secret as it would be serious damage if it is open to customers or to the  European Commission,’’ the
document said.

SE            Business
HD            Electronic firms fined €1.47bn by EU WC           325 words

LP
Royal Philips Electronics and LG Electronics are among companies  fined a record €1.47bn by EU anti-trust regulators over price-fixing  agreements of now-obsolete cathode-ray  tubes used in televisions and computer monitors.
Philips was fined €313.4m while LG faces a €295.6m penalty, said the  European Commission
yesterday.

TD
Philips and LG also share a  fine of €391.9m for a unit they jointly owned.
Panasonic was fined €157.5m and shares an €86.7m punishment with Toshiba and MTPD, a
Panasonic unit. Panasonic and MTPD also share a €7.9m  fine.

Joaquin Almunia, EU competition commissioner, explained in a statement: "Cathode-ray  tubes were a very important component in the making of  television and computer screens. They accounted for
50 to 70% of the price of a screen."

Sales of cathode-ray  tubes fell after customers switched to slimmer liquid- crystal and plasma display sets. Philips and Technicolor, previously Thomson SA, received objections in the EU probe in 2009. Antitrust watchdogs in the EU, Japan, and South Korea raided companies in 2007 over concerns they colluded to fix prices.

Joost Akkermans, spokes- man for Philips, said the  fine was "disproportionate and unjustified" and related to a unit it divested in 2001. Philips will appeal the EU decision, he said.

Samsung SDI Co Ltd, was also told to pay €150.8m. Toshiba was separately  fined €28m and Technicolor SA was  fined €38.6m. Chunghwa Picture  Tubes Ltd was not punished as it was the first to inform regulators of the  cartel.

Top management meetings between the companies to fix prices were often followed by a golf game, the EU said in statement. The firms fixed prices, shared markets, allocated customers between them and restricted their own output in two worldwide cartels between 1996 and 2006.

Samsung SDI, Philips and Technicolor received reductions to their  fines for co-operating with the probe, the EU said.

LP
They met on golf courses across Europe and Asia. Executives from Philips Electronics NV, Panasonic Corp. and five other global electronics companies hatched plans on the links to manipulate prices of television and computer tubes in cartels described by European regulators as among the most organized they had investigated.

A decade of market manipulation in a now-outdated technology -- cathode-ray  tubes for televisions and desktop computers -- drew  fines totaling 1.47 billion euros ($1.92 billion).

TD
The fines, the highest ever handed down in a case by the  European Commission, were the culmination of a three-year probe made in cooperation with other regulators. The market manipulation began in 1996 and ended 10 years later, the European Union said. The EU's previous biggest  fine in a cartel case was 1.38 billion euros, levied in 2008 regarding automobile glass.

The EU said regulators around the globe, including in the U.S., still are investigating the display cartels. The U.S. Justice Department declined to comment on the EU action but confirmed the U.S.
investigation is still open.

Philips Chief Executive Frans van Houten said he regretted "any association" with such behavior. He said the fine, which related to a business sold in 2001, was "disproportionate and unjustified."

Panasonic said the commission's decision was "factually and legally erroneous" and the company would consider its options for appeal to European courts.

The European Commission, which is the EU's enforcement arm, said the  TV- and computer-tube cartels conspired to fix prices and coordinated capacity and production of their products. At their peak, cathode-ray tubes accounted for up to 70% of the price of a  TV set.

"These cartels for cathode-ray  tubes are 'textbook cartels,'" said Joaquin Almunia, the EU's antitrust chief. "They feature all the worst kinds of anticompetitive behavior."

Mr. Almunia said senior executives in the companies referred to their gatherings in Europe and Asia as "green meetings," because they were regularly followed by rounds of golf. The commission was unable to provide more detail on the golf courses but said most of the play took place in Asia, including in Japan and Indonesia.

The deals cut during those encounters were then implemented during more regular, "glass meetings," which Mr. Almunia described as the "engine room of the  cartel," held in cities such as Paris and Hong Kong.

A cartel member boasted that "because of the success of the glass meeting, everybody has been enjoying business this year," according to a document uncovered in the investigation. Another document urged other members to "keep it a secret, as it would be serious damage if it is open to customers and European Commission."
Mr. Almunia said there was "a sophisticated system of monitoring each  cartel participants' behavior."

Page 98 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Demand for cathode-ray  tubes was booming at the start of the  cartel. In 2003, when the technology was replaced by liquid-crystal displays,  cartel members tried to manage the decline, Mr. Almunia told reporters. He said the companies may have "artificially slowed" the transition to plasma displays.
In 2010, the EU  fined six makers of LCD screens 649 million euros for allegedly operating their own
cartel over five years.

Netherlands-based Philips received the highest  fine in the latest case, 313 million euros, for participating in cartels for  TV and computer-monitor tubes. It was ordered to split a 392 million euro fine with LG Electronics Inc. for a joint venture. LG also was ordered to pay 296 million euros for collusion in its own right. Samsung SDI Co. was ordered to pay 151 million euros. The two South Korean companies were found to have participated in both cartels.
LG said it disagrees with the calculation of the  fine and intends to appeal. A spokesman for Samsung
SDI said it hasn't decided whether to appeal.

The commission found that Toshiba Corp., Panasonic and its MTPD subsidiary, and Technicolor SA, formerly Thomson, participated in the  cartel for TV tubes. Panasonic and Toshiba, both of Japan,
were fined 157 million euros and 28 million euros, respectively. MTPD was ordered to pay part of a 95 million euro fine, shared with Panasonic and Toshiba.

France's Technicolor said Thomson sold its cathode-ray tube business in 2005 and the company would record a provision for the  fine. Toshiba didn't immediately comment.

Because it revealed the  cartel's existence, Taiwan-based Chunghwa Picture  Tubes Ltd. received immunity from a  fine that otherwise would have been 17 million, the commission said. Philips and Samsung received reductions in their  fines for cooperating in the investigation. Chunghwa Picture Tubes declined to comment.
"This decision is not the end of the matter," said Caroline Hobson, an antitrust partner for
London-based law firm CMS Cameron McKenna, who wasn't involved in the case. "Customers of the
companies involved will be looking closely at the decision and may result in a number of sizable
damages actions."

The commission said it set the  fines by taking into account the companies' sales and geographic scope, and the severity and duration of the  cartel.

HD                LG, Samsung to appeal EU cartel fines

By Kim Yoo-chul

LG Electronics and Samsung SDI said Thursday that they will appeal heavy  cartel fines imposed by the European Union antitrust regulators for alleged price-rigging on now-obsolete cathode ray tubes (CRT).

TD
Early Thursday (KST), the  European Commission (EC) levied a record-breaking 1.47 billion euro ($1.92 billion) fine on six companies including Samsung and LG, and Toshiba of Japan for colluding to fix prices in two global cartels between 1996 and 2006.

LG Electronics must pay a 295 million euro  fine in addition to a 195 million euro  fine levied on the firm over claims that LG Philips Display (LPD), a CRT unit it once jointly owned, was also involved in the cartel. Samsung SDI must pay 81.4 million euro.

``These cartels for CRTs are textbook cartels. They feature all the worst kinds of anti-competitive behavior that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe,’’ said competition commissioner Joaquin Almunia, adding the price-fixing  scheme harmed European consumers.
In a statement following the EC’s announcement, LG said it will take the case to court before the
February 2013 deadline in an appeal, although the schedule has yet to be decided.
The company pointed out that the EC decision, which ordered it to pay a share of the
now-dissolved joint venture between LG and Philips penalty, doesn’t make any sense as LG
Electronics had divested itself of CRT activities by transferring them to LPD in July 2001.

``LPD had been operated as a totally independent company. It’s true that LG Electronics had previously owned a 50 percent stake in that joint venture. But LG Electronics has no legal responsibility for the penalty. LPD was not consolidated in LG Electronics’ accounts,’’ said spokesman Yoon Won-il. LPD went bankrupt in 2006.

``We have no responsibility to pay any penalties for alleged price-fixing  cartels concerning our CRT activities before the establishment of LPD because the reason is simple _ under EU competition law, companies have no legal responsibility to pay anything if the investigation is conducted five years after such activities ceased,’’ said Yoon.
The EU started its investigation of the six companies for price-fixing  in 2007.

According to LG, the world’s second-biggest  TV producer, anti-trust watchdogs in Korea, Japan, Canada, the United States and the Czech Republic cleared the company of being involved in a price-fixing cartel and decided not to impose  fines.

Samsung SDI said that it will also protest the EU decision. ``SDI will appeal and implement further legal measures if needed,’’ said spokesman Seo Hae-su.

zz Philips, LG among firms penalised e1.47bn for price  fixing

ROYAL Philips Electronics and LG Electronics are among companies that have been  fined a record e1.47 billion (R16.8bn) by EU antitrust regulators over price-fixing  agreements for now-obsolete cathode ray tubes used in television and computer monitors.

TD
Philips had been  fined e313.4 million while LG faced a e295.6m penalty, the European Commission said in a statement yesterday. The companies also shared a fine of e391.9m for a unit they jointly owned.
Panasonic was fined e157.5m and shared an e86.7m punishment with Toshiba and MTPD, a
Panasonic unit.

Philips spokesman Joost Akkermans said the  fine was "disproportionate and unjustified" and related to a unit it divested in 2001. Philips would appeal the EU decision, he said.

"Cathode ray  tubes were a very important component in the making of  television and computer screens. They accounted for 50 percent to 70 percent of the price of a screen," EU Competition Commissioner Joaquin Almunia said.

Sales of cathode ray  tubes used in television and computer monitors declined after customers switched to liquid-crystal and plasma display sets.

Philips and Technicolor, previously known as Thomson, received objections in the EU probe in 2009. Antitrust watchdogs in the EU, Japan and South Korea raided companies in 2007 over concerns they had colluded to fix prices.

Samsung SDI, an affiliate of Samsung Electronics, was also told to pay e150.8m. Toshiba was separately fined e28m and Technicolor was  fined e38.6m.

Samsung SDI, Philips and Technicolor received reductions to their  fines for co-operating with the investigation.
Chunghwa Picture Tubes was not punished because it was the first to inform regulators of the  cartel. Top management meetings between the companies to fix prices were often followed by a game of
golf, the EU said in the statement. The firms fixed prices, shared markets, allocated customers
between them and restricted their own output in two worldwide cartels between 1996 and 2006.

"These cartels for cathode ray  tubes are 'textbook cartels': they feature all the worst kinds of anti-competitive behaviour that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe," Almunia said.

He said the violations were especially harmful for consumers, as cathode ray  tubes accounted for between 50 percent and 70 percent of the price of a screen.
Page 106 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Cathode ray tubes have largely been replaced by more advanced display technologies such as liquid-crystal display, plasma display and organic light-emitting diodes.

The biggest  fine prior to the cathode ray  tubes cartel was e1.38bn imposed on participants in a motor vehicle glass cartel in 2008.

The commission's sanctions followed a total  fine of e128.74m levied last year against four producers of the glass used in cathode ray  tubes.

Chunghwa Picture Tubes, Samsung Electronics, LG Display and three other liquid crystal display producers were fined a total e648m two years ago for taking part in a  cartel.

HD     'Textbook' CRT 'Cartels'  Slapped with Largest EU Antitrust Fines
WC    590 words
PD     6 December 2012
SN     Warren's Consumer Electronics Daily
SC     CEDW
VOL   Volume 12; Issue 235
LA     English
CY     (c) Copyright 2012 Warren Publishing, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

LP
The European Commission slapped seven producers of  TV and computer monitor tubes collectively with a 1.47 billion-euro ($1.9 billion)  fine for engaging in two decades of price-fixing,  it said Wednesday (http://xrl.us/bn4yhy). These were "textbook cartels" featuring "all the worst kinds" of anticompetitive behavior, said Competition Commissioner Joaqu&iacuten Almunia. Philips said it will appeal, while other makers didn't comment or couldn't be reached. A European consumer group said the case shows the need for class-action lawsuits.

TD
There were two distinct cartels, among the most organized the EC has investigated, it said. From
1996-2006, the companies set prices, shared markets, allocated customers among themselves and
restricted output, it said. One  cartel related to CRTs used for TVs, the other to CRTs for  computer
monitors. The collusive relationship operated worldwide, it said. The cartels have also been probed in
Korea and Japan, and the U.S. Department of Justice is investigating, Almunia said.

Chunghwa, LG Electronics, Philips and Samsung SDI took part in both cartels Panasonic, Toshiba, MTPD (a Panasonic subsidiary) and Technicolor (formerly Thomson) only in the one for TV tubes, the EC said. Chunghwa, the first to disclose the cartels' existence to the EC, received full immunity. Fines for others were reduced according to their level of cooperation, it said.
When the collusion began, demand for CRTs was booming, Almunia said at a press briefing. But from
2003, when CRTS were being increasingly replaced by flat-panel screens, the cartels' actions meant
consumers were paying more since CRTs account for 50-70 percent of a screen price, he said. The
companies were trying to address the decline of the CRT market collusively, he said. One document said
"producers need to avoid price competition through controlling their production capacity."  Cartel policies
were sometimes set by top managers during "green(s) meetings" on golf courses, and meetings were held
in Asia and Europe, he said. The antitrust probe showed that the companies knew they were breaking the
law. One document, which Almunia read out, said "everybody is requested to keep it as secret as it would
be serious damage if it is open to customers or  European Commission."

The collusion made victims not only of companies that bought the CRTs but also of consumers, Almunia said. Private damage actions are permitted, but individuals may not have the same opportunities to file them as larger players, he said. The EC would like to see private enforcement complementing its action, he said.

Philips was fined 313 million euros individually and another 392 million euros jointly with LG Electronics for antitrust violations by the now-defunct 50-50 joint venture, LG.Philips Displays, Philips said Wednesday. Philips divested its CRT business by transferring it to LG.Philips in 2001, it said. "We regret any association with this type of behavior," said CEO Frans van Houten. But the  fine, related to a business that was divested in 2001, "is disproportionate and unjustified," he said. The company will appeal, it said.

The record fine "is only half a solution," European Consumers' Organisation Director General Monique Goyens. Almunia is expected to say in coming weeks whether a collective redress measure will be introduced, she said. "Not one euro cent will be seen by the most important parties to this -- the victims themselves," she said. The  TV cartel "needs to be final proof" that a system for collective private damages claims is long overdue, she said. -- Dugie Standeford

HD     (AE) COMPETITION: Record €1.47 billion fine on TV and computer cartels

LP
ECONOMY - FINANCE - BUSINESS



TD
Brussels, 05/12/2012 (Agence Europe) - On 5 December, the  European Commission levied record fines of €1.47 billion - the highest yet - on seven international electronics companies for their decade-long involvement in one or two global cartels in cathode ray  tubes for colour televisions and  computer monitors.

From 1996 to 2006, Chunghwa, Samsung SDI, Philips, LG Electronics, Technicolor (ex-Thomson), Panasonic, Toshiba and MTPD (now a subsidiary of Panasonic) agreed on prices and the distribution of markets and customers and restrictions on their manufacture of cathode ray  tubes for televisions. The first four companies listed were involved in both cartels.

Top management level meetings, dubbed “green(s) meetings” by the cartelists themselves because they were often followed by a golf game, designed the orientations for the two cartels. Preparation and implementation were carried out through lower level meetings, often referred to as “glass meetings”, on a quarterly, monthly, sometimes even weekly basis in various locations in Asia and Europe. The cartels operated worldwide and enabled the companies to set and maintain high prices and protect the cathode ray tube TV market by slowing the transition to flat and LCD screens.

In setting the  fines, the Commission took into account the companies' sales of the products concerned in the EEA, the very serious nature of the infringement, its geographic scope, its implementation and its duration, fining the first cartel a total of €1.14 billion (TV tubes) and €3.28 billion in total for the second cartel (computer monitor tubes). Chunghwa was given full immunity for informing the Commission about the cartel, but Philips has to pay a total of €313 million for its involvement in both cartels, and a further fine of €392 million to be paid in combination with LG Electronics. Philips was granted a 30% reduction, and Samsung a 40% reduction of its €151 million  fine for their cooperation under the Commission's leniency programme. Technicolor was granted a 10% reduction on its €39 million  fine because of its inability to pay the  fine due to its poor financial health. The  cartel is also under investigation in South Korea, Japan and the United States. (FG/transl.fl)

Philips, LG Electronics, Samsung SDI and three other firms were  fined a record 1.47 billion euros ($1.92 billion) by EU antitrust regulators for  fixing  prices of TV and monitor cathode-ray  tubes for nearly a decade.
The European Commission slapped the biggest penalty, of 313.4 million euros, on Dutch-based
Philips on Wednesday. LG Electronics came in second with a 295.6 million euro  fine.

TD
The EU competition authority  finedPanasonic Corp 157.5 million euros, Samsung SDI 150.8 million euros, Toshiba Corp 28 million euros, and French firm Technicolor 38.6 million euros.

Two Panasonic joint ventures were also fined. Taiwanese firm Chunghwa Picture  Tubes blew the whistle on the cartels in  TV and computer monitors and escaped a  fine.

The two cartels operated worldwide between 1996 and 2006, during which company executives discussed how to fix prices and share markets at "green meetings", so-called because the events often ended with a round of golf.
"These cartels for cathode ray  tubes are 'textbook cartels': they feature all the worst kinds of
anti-competitive behaviour that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe," EU
Competition Commissioner Joaquin Almunia said.

He said the violations were harmful for consumers, as cathode ray  tubes accounted for 50-70 per cent of the price of a screen.

HD     EU imposes  $1.9 billion cartel fine on screen producers including Philips,  LG, Panasonic

BY      By RAF CASERT CR     Associated Press WC     844 words
PD      6 December 2012
ET      02:02 AM
SN      Associated Press Newswires
SC      APRS
LA      English
CY      (c) 2012. The Associated Press. All Rights Reserved.

LP
BRUSSELS (AP) - For years, some top managers at electronics multinationals would hold secretive meetings in Europe and Asia to fix the global market on the most expensive part of  television and computer monitors.

Then, said the European Union's antitrust chief, it was off to the golf course, where the executives would complete the "green(s) meetings" at which they set up a massive scam that affected millions of consumers.

TD
On Wednesday, EU antitrust Commissioner Joaquin Almunia said he got back at them, slapping the biggest ever cartel fine -- over (EURO)1.47 billion ($1.96 billion) -- on seven companies for allegedly rigging the international market of  television and computer monitor tubes.

The EU's Commission ruled that, for a decade ending in 2006, the companies -- including Philips, LG Electronics and Panasonic -- artificially set prices, shared markets and restricted their output.

Almunia said the companies' actions "feature all the worst kinds of anticompetitive behavior that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe."

Tubes were the essential part of  television screens and computer monitors before they were replaced by LCD and plasma flatscreens. The cathode ray  tubes accounted for up to 70 percent of the cost of a screen, the Commission said.
Almunia added that the  tubes' cost gave "an indication of the serious harm" the companies had caused.
`'There are victims: millions and millions of citizens."

Philips and LG Electronics, which acted jointly and separately, were  fined a combined (EURO)999 million ($1.3 billion) with Panasonic punished with a (EURO)157 million ($205 million)  fine, adding to more if combined fines and affiliates were included.
Despite its cooperation with the Commission probe, Philips said in a statement it planned to appeal the
fine since it considered it `'disproportionate and unjust."

"We regret that we are linked to this kind of behavior," said Philips CEO Frans van Houten, as the company spoke of `'alleged infringements of competition rules."

LG Electronics said it is currently reviewing the  European Commission's decision with the intention to appeal the decision.

The South Korean  TV maker argued that it should not be held liable for the conduct of LG Philips Display, a joint venture between LG and Royal Philips Electronics NV.
LG also disagreed with the way the Commission calculated the amount of  fine.



Page 112 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
"It appears that the  European Commission has calculated the  fine imposed on LG Electronics partly on the basis of  TV sets and PC monitors sold by LG Electronics in Europe rather than just cathode ray tubes," LG said in a statement.

Other companies  fined were Samsung SDI, Technicolor, MTPD and Toshiba. Chunghwa of Taiwan escaped fines as it was the first to reveal the  cartel to the EU.
`'It is the biggest  fine for a cartel, ever," Almunia said. The fine exceeded the previous record of the 2008
fine of (EURO)1.38 billion ($1.8 billion) in a car glass cartel.

Almunia said the  tubes cartel operated worldwide and that the companies involved fully realized they acted illegally.

After top management set the strategy at Green(s) meetings, lower-level company officials discussed the practical applications of the price-fixing  scheme during so-called `'Glass meetings," referring to the core material of the  tubes.

`'The participants during this period seemed very happy about the outcome," Almunia said, citing one document that read: `'Because of the success of the `glass meeting' everybody has been enjoying business this year." Not the consumer, he said.

The cartel started when the tube market was still booming but lasted even when sales dropped with the advent of flatscreens. The  cartel made sure the companies kept making profits even as demand for tube screens waned.

Almunia said the companies sought to keep prices as high as possible before new technologies came to the market. One document seized by the Commission gave the conspiring companies clear instructions: "Producers need to avoid price competition through controlling their production capacity."

A separate document had a warning: "Everybody is requested to keep it as secret as it would be serious damage if it is open to customers or  European Commission." Another said: "Please dispose the following document after reading it."

`'Instead of competing with each other to innovate and provide the best product at the best price, they chose to conspire to artificially maintain returns in a declining technology market," Almunia said. `'This is why we fight cartels."

He said the television and computer cartels were among the most sophisticated the EU had ever dealt with. He said they not only fixed prices, but also shared markets, coordinated capacity, output and customer allocation. In the  computer monitor cartel, they even audited compliance.

The Commission started its probe with an antitrust raid on the companies in November 2007. Almunia said U.S. authorities were currently investigating the case as well.
------
Mike Corder in The Hague and Sam Kim in Seoul contributed to this article.

HD      LG Elec. mulls appealing  EU penalty
WC     259 words
PD      6 December 2012
SN      Maeil Business Newspaper
SC      MAEIL
LA       English
CY      Copyright 2012 MAEKYUNG.COM Inc.

LP
South Korea's LG Electronics said Thursday it plans to appeal the  European Commission's 491.6 million euro fine for its alleged price-rigging of cathode ray  tubes (CRTs).

The company said it will closely review the decision and appeal it to a European court, adding that the European regulator imposed the penalty by simply including CRT sales in Europe in its penalty calculation without proving how the alleged  cartel affected the European  TV and monitor market.

TD
Earlier, the commission  fined a group of seven international companies, including LG Electronics, Philips Electronics, Panasonic and Samsung, a total of 1.47 billion euros for price  fixing  of CRT in two global cartels between 1996 and 2006.

The commission slapped a 295.6 million euro  fine on LG Electronics and an additional  fine of 392 million euros to be shared between Philips and LG for LG Philips LCD, a unit once jointly owned with the Dutch company.

LG Electronics said LG Philips LCD was an independent unity and it does not hold responsible for it. LG Electronics transferred all of its CRT business to the joint venture when it was set up in July 2001. In
2006, LG Philips LCD went bankrupt.
[Written by Gye-man Kang / edited by Soyoung Chung]

SE          Business/Financial Desk; SECTB
HD          Europe Strikes Tech Cartels With Fine of $1.96 Billion
BY          By JAMES KANTER
WC         950 words
PD          6 December 2012
SN          The New York Times
SC          NYTF
ED          Late Edition - Final
PG          3
LA           English
CY          Copyright 2012 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

LP
BRUSSELS -- For a decade, senior managers at some of the world's largest electronics companies met at golf courses, mostly in Asia, for what they called ''greens meetings.''

Besides golf, the business at hand was a price-fixing  scheme that affected millions of consumers, the top European antitrust regulator said on Wednesday.

TD
Joaquín Almunia, the European competition commissioner, imposed  fines totaling almost 1.5 billion euros, or $1.96 billion, on seven companies involved in two cartels that fixed the price of picture and display tubes for televisions and  computer screens.

Combined, the  fines amount to the largest single penalty for price  fixing  ever imposed by the commission, which said the cartels had engaged in the most organized market manipulation it had ever investigated. In addition to price  fixing,  the cartels' conspiracies included market sharing, customer allocation and exchanges of important commercial information.

The action follows a spate of similar cases in the glass and display sectors, where bulky cathode ray tubes have been supplanted by technologies like liquid-crystal display and plasma that allow manufacturers to build far more compact monitors and screens.
But starting in the late 1990s, when the market was still strong for cathode ray  tubes, and lasting until
2006, the conspirators' scheme allowed them to continue generating strong returns for a technology
that was rapidly becoming outmoded.

''The companies were trying to manage through collusion the decline in the market for these kinds of tubes,'' Mr. Almunia said at a news conference. ''The undue profits that the companies derived from the collusion may even have artificially slowed down the transition to the more modern products like LCD and plasma displays.''

Excerpts from minutes from meetings held by the  cartel members obtained during the investigation showed the efforts they made to fix the market for the older technologies, according to commission officials.

''Producers need to avoid price competition through controlling their production capacity (of flat types in particular),'' one excerpt read. Another noted that ''mutual cooperation is required to deal with an expected economic downturn'' in the second half of 2002.

In addition to the ''greens meetings,'' there were ''glass meetings'' for lower-level managers. The name probably related to the glass structure of the cathode ray  tubes, officials said. They were held in Asia and in European cities including Glasgow, Paris, Rome, Amsterdam and Budapest, commission officials said.

The cartels ''feature all the worst kinds of anticompetitive behavior that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe,'' Mr. Almunia said. Producers in Europe and consumers suffered
Page 116 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
serious harm, he said, because the cathode ray  tubes accounted for as much as 70 percent of the price of screens.

During his news conference, Mr. Almunia read from one of the documents obtained by the commission to show that  cartel members were aware they were breaking the law. ''Everybody is requested to keep it a secret,'' the document read, ''as it would be serious damage if it is open to customers or to the European Commission.''

The commission's antitrust division can  fine offenders as much as 10 percent of their annual worldwide sales, and the  fine on Wednesday exceeded the previous record of almost 1.4 billion euros, which was imposed upon an auto-glass  cartel in 2008.

But unlike regulators in the United States, the commission has no criminal enforcement powers and cannot prosecute or seek to jail participants for anticompetitive offenses. Many lawyers say this lack remains a shortcoming of the European system.

''There is a deterrent effect as these  fines get higher, but there are always going to be some companies with wayward commercial personnel and some companies where some people say, 'Let's take the risk as might be worth it in the long run,' '' said Caroline Hobson, a competition law partner at CMS Cameron McKenna in London. ''An even greater enforcement power is the power to jail offenders, because that really makes people wake up.''
She said she did not represent any of the companies involved in the case.

Mr. Almunia imposed the harshest penalties on Royal Philips Electronics of the Netherlands and LG Electronics of South Korea. Philips was  fined 313.4 million euros and LG Electronics 295.6 million euros. Philips and LG also were also part of a joint venture that received an additional  fine of 391.9 million euros for which both companies were liable.

The commission also finedPanasonic 157.5 million euros, Samsung 150.8 million euros, Toshiba 28 million euros and Technicolor 38.6 million euros.

In a statement, Philips said it would appeal the  fines to the General Court of the European Union, the bloc's second-highest tribunal. Philips described the  fine relating to its involvement in the joint venture as ''disproportionate and unjustified.'' Philips said it had divested that business in 2001.
LG did not respond to an e-mail request for comment.

The sanctions announced on Wednesday were the latest handed down by the commission over activity in the sector. It levied a fine of 128.7 million euros last year against four producers of the glass that is used in cathode ray tubes, and it imposed a 649 million euro  fine in 2010 against members of a  cartel that fixed prices on flat-panel displays based on LCD technology. One of the companies involved in
that case, Chunghwa from Taiwan, reported the picture  tubes case to the commission. As a result, Chunghwa received full immunity from  fines.

ART        An LG Electronics  television plant in 2006. LG faces huge penalties for picture-tube price  fixing. (PHOTOGRAPH BY SCOTT EELLS FOR THE NEW YORK TIMES)

HD          Europe Fines Electronics Makers $1.92 Billion

SE           BRE
HD           EU slaps record fine on TV tube cartel
WC          500 words
PD           6 December 2012
SN           The Star SC           THESTR ED           E1
PG           8
LA           English
CY           © 2012 Independent Newspapers (Pty) Ltd

LP
zz Philips, LG among firms penalised e1.47bn for price  fixing

ROYAL Philips Electronics and LG Electronics are among companies that have been  fined a record e1.47 billion (R16.8bn) by EU antitrust regulators over price-fixing  agreements for now-obsolete cathode ray tubes used in television and computer monitors.

TD
Philips had been  fined e313.4 million while LG faced a e295.6m penalty, the  European Commission said in a statement yesterday. The companies also shared a  fine of e391.9m for a unit they jointly owned.
Panasonic was fined e157.5m and shared an e86.7m punishment with Toshiba and MTPD, a
Panasonic unit.

Philips spokesman Joost Akkermans said the  fine was "disproportionate and unjustified" and related to a unit it divested in 2001. Philips would appeal the EU decision, he said.

"Cathode ray  tubes were a very important component in the making of  television and computer screens. They accounted for 50 percent to 70 percent of the price of a screen," EU Competition Commissioner Joaquin Almunia said.

Sales of cathode ray  tubes used in television and computer monitors declined after customers switched to liquid-crystal and plasma display sets.

Philips and Technicolor, previously known as Thomson, received objections in the EU probe in 2009. Antitrust watchdogs in the EU, Japan and South Korea raided companies in 2007 over concerns they had colluded to fix prices.

Samsung SDI, an affiliate of Samsung Electronics, was also told to pay e150.8m. Toshiba was separately fined e28m and Technicolor was  fined e38.6m.

Samsung SDI, Philips and Technicolor received reductions to their  fines for co-operating with the investigation.
Chunghwa Picture  Tubes was not punished because it was the first to inform regulators of the  cartel. Top management meetings between the companies to fix prices were often followed by a game of
golf, the EU said in the statement. The firms fixed prices, shared markets, allocated customers
between them and restricted their own output in two worldwide cartels between 1996 and 2006.
"These cartels for cathode ray  tubes are 'textbook cartels': they feature all the worst kinds of
anti-competitive behaviour that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe," Almunia
said.

He said the violations were especially harmful for consumers, as cathode ray  tubes accounted for between 50 percent and 70 percent of the price of a screen.
Page 120 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
Cathode ray tubes have largely been replaced by more advanced display technologies such as liquid-crystal display, plasma display and organic light-emitting diodes.

The biggest fine prior to the cathode ray  tubes cartel was e1.38bn imposed on participants in a motor vehicle glass cartel in 2008.

The commission's sanctions followed a total  fine of e128.74m levied last year against four producers of the glass used in cathode ray  tubes.

Chunghwa Picture Tubes, Samsung Electronics, LG Display and three other liquid crystal display producers were fined a total e648m two years ago for taking part in a  cartel.

HD          Toshiba:  To Appeal European Commission Ruling on Monitor  Tube Price-Fixing
BY           By Hiroyuki Kachi

TOKYO--Toshiba Corp. (6502.TO) said Thursday that it intends to appeal the European
Commission's decision to  fine the company for  fixing  the prices of television tubes.

TD
The Japanese company is among seven companies fined a record 1.47 billion euros ($1.92 billion) by
European regulators Wednesday for operating price-fixing  cartels for cathode ray  tubes.
Our investigation showed we didn't take action that violated competition law in Europe and we intend
to fight by bringing the latest case to the European Union's General Court of Justice," Toshiba said in a
statement.
Toshiba said the European CommissionfinedToshiba itself a total EUR28 million (Y3.0 billion). Separately, it said the  European Commission imposed a EUR87 million  fine on Toshiba's former
joint venture with Panasonic Corp. (6752.TO), to be shared between the two companies.
Toshiba also said it hasn't change its earnings outlook for this fiscal year ending March. Write to Hiroyuki Kachi at Hiroyuki.Kachi@dowjones.com

TD

HD           TV Makers fined GBP1.2bn for price-fix cartel
BY           Claire Smith
WC         457 words
PD          6 December 2012
SN          The Scotsman
SC          SC
ED          4
PG          11
LA           English
CY          (c) 2012

LP
A CARTEL of international companies was fined €1.5 billion (GBP1.2bn) yesterday for  fixing  the market in television and computer monitor tubes.
The price of a key part in the manufacture of televisions and computers was rigged for a decade by a
cartel of huge companies, it has been revealed.

TD
Seven companies, including Philips, LG and Panasonic, were  fined by the European Commission after an investigation into anti-competitive practices. Top executives met secretly to fix the price of cathode ray tubes at "green" meetings - which were rounded off by a game of golf.

At lower-level but equally secretive "glass" meetings around the world, executives from the electronics manufacturers conspired to keep the price of cathode ray  tubes unnaturally high - thereby inflating the cost of televisions and computers to millions of consumers.

Commission vice-president in charge of competition policy JoaquÃn Almunia said: "Cathode ray  tubes were a very important component in the making of  television and computer screens. They accounted for 50 to 70 per cent of the price of a screen.

"This gives an indication of the serious harm this illegal behaviour has caused both to  television and computer screen producers, and ultimately the harm it caused to the European consumers over the years."

Mr Almunia said: "There are victims: millions and millions of citizens. These cartels for cathode ray tubes are 'textbook cartels' - they feature all the worst kinds of anti-competitive behaviour that are strictly forbidden to companies doing business in Europe."

Other companies  fined were Samsung SDI, Technicolour, MTPD and Toshiba. Chunghwa of Taiwan escaped fines as it was the first to reveal the  cartel to the EU.
"It is the biggest  fine for a cartel, ever," Mr Almunia said.

He said the price-fixing cartel operated worldwide with the companies involved being fully aware they were acting illegally.

One document seized by the commission warned executives: "Everybody is requested to keep it as secret as it would be serious damage if it is open to customers or  European Commission." Another said: "Please dispose the following document after reading it."

"Instead of competing with each other to innovate and provide the best product at the best price, they chose to conspire to artificially maintain returns," Mr Almunia said. "This is why we fight cartels."

Philips said in a statement it planned to appeal the  fine since it considered it "disproportionate and unjust".


Page 135 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
"We regret that we are linked to this kind of behaviour," said chief executive Frans van Houten. LG Electronics said it would only react after studying the decision.

In a statement, the EC said: "The cartels are among the most organised that the commission has investigated."
CO          eucmm : European Commission
NS          eucom : European Commission | eec : European Union | ocat : International Pol-Econ Organizations

RE          scot : Scotland | eecz : European Union Countries | eurz : Europe | uk : United Kingdom | weurz : Western Europe
PUB        The Scotsman Publications Limited
AN          Document SC00000020121206e8c60000q
























































Page 136 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.



SE          Business
HD         Electronics cartel 'pushed  TV prices higher for decade' BY          Alex Spence
WC         497 words
PD          6 December 2012
SN          The Times
SC          T
ED          1; National
PG         54
LA          English
CY          © 2012 Times Newspapers Ltd. All rights reserved

LP
They called them "green meetings" — secret huddles of some of the world's top electronics executives at which they carved up the cathode ray  tubes market and then hit the golf course.

The clandestine meetings, involving executives from LG, Philips, Samsung and Panasonic, laid the foundations for one of the most egregious cartels that European antitrust investigators have come across, it was alleged yesterday.

TD
Seven electronics manufacturers were fined a record €1.5 billion by the European Commission for rigging the market for cathode ray tubes used in televisions and computer monitors. The manufacturers operated two cartels for almost a decade and were knowingly breaking the law, Europe's top competition regulator alleged.

Investigators from the Commission uncovered documents in which the conspirators apparently brazenly discussed the need to keep their discussions secret.

"Everybody is requested to keep as secret as it would be serious damage if it is open to customers or the European Commission," one warned. Another stated: "Please dispose the following document after reading it."

The enforcement action — the biggest by the  European Commission in a cartel case — could open the electronics giants to legal action by consumers who were overcharged as a result of the alleged collusion.

Philips described its penalty as "disproportionate and unjustified", because its tube production unit was divested in 2001, and said that it would appeal against the Commission's decision. Panasonic said that the decision was "factually and legally erroneous" and also said that it would consider an appeal.

The Commission's investigation into the alleged cartels began with a series of raids in November 2007. Cathode ray tubes were an important component in the making of  television and computer screens, accounting for up to 70 per cent of the price, the regulator said.

It believes that screen manufacturers and, ultimately, consumers were overcharged for almost a decade as a result of the tube producers conspiring to distort the market. The Commission said: "For almost ten years, the cartelists carried out the most harmful anti-competitive practices including
price-fixing,  market-sharing, customer allocation, capacity and output co-ordination and exchanges of commercially sensitive information."

The cartels operated worldwide, dictated by executives who drew up the plans at the "green meetings". The implementation of the schemes were left to their lieutenants to work out at other meetings in cities such as Hong Kong, Amsterdam, Paris and Rome, the Commission alleges.

The biggest  fines were against Philips and LG, which were involved in both cartels. They received penalties of €313.4 million and €295.6 million, respectively. Samsung, also involved in both cartels,

Page 137 of 177 © 2019 Factiva, Inc. All rights  reserved.
was fined €150.8 million. Panasonic and Toshiba, which participated only in the  television tubes        cartel, were hit with penalties of €157.5 million and €28 million, respectively. Smaller companies were also fined, bringing the total to €1.47 billion.

Chunghwa, a Taiwanese manufacturer, received immunity because it blew the whistle on the price-fixing.
